He lifted his chin slightly. 'Nobody comes up Hanover Street just looking,' he said. 'Find out what you came for. Then come back when you know.'

He turned back into the shop. The measuring tape swung once against his chest and was still.

Miriam glanced at her sideways. 'He says that to everyone,' she said. 'The first time.'

Ruth looked back at the doorway. The tailor was already at his table, his head bent over a length of dark cloth, the cigarette still burning in the ashtray beside him. Through the window above his workbench she saw a child's drawing tacked to the wall: a house in blue crayon, a sun with straight lines radiating from it, the lines very even and very careful.

The tailor had done in two sentences what most of her tutorials required an hour not to do. Not welcome, not dismissal. A direction and a standing condition: come back when you know. She had not been addressed that precisely since the convent. But the convent's precision had been a correction. This was something else.

She turned back to the street. The wind off the mountain reached her here between the buildings, carrying the smell of the sea and the roasting mealies and something else underneath, something she could not name and did not try to.

Miriam walked on without looking back.

The lane they turned into was barely wide enough for two people side by side. The buildings on each flank rose close enough overhead that the iron balconies nearly met, their railings strung with washing and bicycles and in one case a birdcage with the door left open and the occupant long gone. The sound changed when they entered: harder, more contained, the cobblestones returning a different report underfoot than the wide street had, and the cold in the lane was not the mobile cold of moving air but the cold of stone that had held the sea's moisture for weeks and had no particular reason to release it.

Three shops along, a barber's pole turned in a doorway. The red and the white had gone grey in the shadow, the motor driving the rotation old enough that its movement had slowed to the pace of something considering. The Dutch door stood open at the top and shut at the bottom, and from inside came not a voice but several voices at different registers, running in parallel, none of them requiring the others to stop while they spoke: a voice attending to the man in the chair, a voice addressing someone at the counter, a third voice addressing the street at large, making an observation about the morning that nobody was required to answer.

'Miriam,' the voice said, without pause.

Miriam stopped at the doorway. 'Uncle Jonas.'

The man with the razor , short, carefully moustached, apron knotted with the economy of someone for whom the motion is entirely automatic , did not look up from the neck he was attending. 'The scholarship girl too. Nadia, chairs.'

Inside, the shop was long and low-ceilinged. The smell of it was layered: bay rum at the immediate surface, then talc, then beneath both the sweet, slightly rancid note of brilliantine applied in summer heat and still present in the winter walls, the record of the same transactions performed across a great many years. The mirror ran the length of the room, and in it Ruth saw herself and Miriam and Jonas and the customer and the girl behind the counter and the whole interior doubled, the actual proportions of the space extended beyond their actual dimensions. Above the mirror, photographs covered the wall: men in various conditions of haircut at various points in their lives, christenings and weddings and events whose occasions she could not immediately read, each annotated in a small, confident Afrikaans hand. *Van Niekerk-trouery. Reunie 1936.* A rugby match with a hand-painted banner just visible at one edge. Two photographs pinned close together: a man in a South African forces uniform, and beside it what appeared to be the same man twenty years later, seated at a kitchen table, his eyes at a point slightly above the camera lens and not at it.

The tea arrived in two cups without saucers, carried by Nadia who was perhaps fourteen and whose preoccupied air suggested her mind was already attending to something outside this room and this errand. Strong enough to stand a spoon in. Ruth held the cup in both hands.

When his customer was paid and gone, Jonas took the empty chair at the end of the row. He folded his hands on his apron with the deliberate ease of a man who makes few gestures and keeps each one legible.

'You're from the north,' he said to Ruth.

'Near Tzaneen. Lowveld.'

'Long way south.'

'Yes.'

'You staying?'

She considered it. The question had the specific gravity of a man who has asked it before and received a variety of answers and is still collecting them. 'I think so,' she said.

He looked at the far end of the mirror. Behind the reflections of the two of them the photographs receded in the glass, the faces slightly smaller, the inscriptions illegible. 'This district stays too,' he said. 'Also not certain about it. Also saying it for too long.' He stood and unwound the apron from his waist, folded it with the particular efficiency of someone who places everything in an exact position because the space requires it. 'There's a notice board on Caledon Street this morning. Same language as the last one. A little more specific about the timeline. Come back when you want a proper cup.'

He had a new customer in the chair before they had returned their cups to the counter.

---

They walked north from Caledon Street through the side streets, Miriam navigating with the orientation of someone who learned these directions long enough ago that the learning had worn away and only the result remained. She knew which lane cut cleanly through to the next street and which only appeared to, which courtyard was public passage and which was a building's private yard, which corner-shop owner would allow two people to stand in his doorway out of the wind without buying anything as long as they kept themselves to the left of the display. She pointed and named as they went, not for Ruth's benefit in particular and not explaining anything: the secondary school, the fire station that had been a church hall before the fire station, a building she indicated only by a tilt of her head whose significance Ruth could not read.

A hardware merchant's door stood open and the smell of turpentine and sawdust came out of it into the lane, a clean, sharp smell after the damp stone. Inside a man was cutting a length of timber on a board, the saw moving with the smooth, considering stroke of someone who has done this long enough that he no longer thinks about the cut but only about where it needs to end.

Further along, a butcher and a fishmonger occupied adjacent shops, their doors both open, their voices both carrying at full volume, each one calling his prices in the tone and register of a man who considers that his product's merit speaks for itself but considers also that it speaks more forcefully when amplified. The butcher called in Afrikaans. The fishmonger called in English and then repeated the same sentence in Afrikaans, as if offering both options were a courtesy the street deserved. The smell of the fish overlay the smell of the sawdust, and both were overlaid by a smell of something frying from the window of the house above, and the compound of all three was the smell of that hour on that street, entirely its own.

At the top of the slope a group of boys had taken possession of the lane's lower section for a game of football whose rules appeared to be subject to continuous renegotiation. The argument about the rules was being conducted in three languages, or what presented as three but moved between them so fluidly that the boundaries were more a matter of temperature than category, the Afrikaans cooling into formal English and then the English warming back into something that was neither and was used between them with the ease of a private syntax. The ball went into the gutter. Someone disputed whose turn it was to retrieve it. The language of this dispute was briefly all three at once.

A man in a white kufi came down the far side of the lane from the mosque above the street, moving at a pace that had purpose in it. He crossed to the sunny side near the hardware shop. A man in a dark coat came out of the hardware merchant's door at the same moment, a length of wood under one arm, and looked up. The first man said a name. The second man said a name in return, his hand coming up briefly. They did not stop. Each continued in his direction and the street absorbed both of them.

Ruth walked through this and said nothing.

From a second-storey window above the lane an accordion began, its bass notes carrying better than its reeds in the cold air, playing a melody Ruth half-recognised, something she had heard from a gramophone somewhere in a room she could not immediately place. Two women were standing on the stoep below it. They had come out of the house's front door and were on the pavement, either waiting for someone or simply standing, and they had begun singing along with the accordion melody in a quiet, domestic way, both of them getting the words slightly wrong and neither of them minding. They were still singing, or approximately singing, when Ruth and Miriam reached the corner and turned north.

---

The music came from above, from a window propped open with something folded against the cold. A penny-whistle running a melodic phrase in a repeating loop, not seeking a resolution, going around as if the point were the going rather than the arrival. And under it a guitar picking a rhythm in a different time, and the two converged at the end of every phrase as if they had an arrangement that dispensed with everything in between, arriving in the same place at the same count through different routes.

Miriam led her through an arched entrance into a courtyard. The arch was worn enough at the keystone to predate everything else in the street by a generation. The courtyard held the enclosed smell of coal smoke and old cooking and the sweet fermentation note of fruit going over in a warm interior, the compound smell of a space whose walls had been absorbing the record of daily life for a long time and did not intend to stop.

A boy of about fifteen was in the courtyard mending a bicycle with total, undisturbable concentration. He did not look up.

The stairwell was narrow, the walls a buff yellow going darker toward the top where the air did not move. On the first landing a watercolour was pinned above the door: the mountain from a viewpoint below, filling the upper half of the paper, the rooftops of the district below it dense and ordinary and unrealised. The painter had not added anything to either the buildings or the mountain that was not observationally necessary. Both were present in the painting the way things are present in fact: without invitation and without apology.

On the second floor, four men were playing cards around a cable spool stood on its end. The cards moved between them with the automatic speed of a game well past requiring thought. An argument was running simultaneously in at least three languages in the undertone beneath the card game, not interrupting it, the argument as ambient as the light in the room: something about Joseph, something about Joseph's analysis of a situation, something about the gap between Joseph's analysis and the situation. One man, grey at the temples, in a jacket that had been a good jacket some years past, looked up at Ruth.

'New face,' he said. His eyes went to Ruth.

'She reads Latin,' Ruth said.

He looked at the man across the spool. Something moved between them, not any readable expression, only the density of a private understanding. He played his card. 'Tell her we'll be needing it,' he said, 'when the university finishes sorting out our address problem.' The man to his left assessed the played card with the compressed misgiving of someone who finds a legal move philosophically inadvisable.

They climbed to the third floor.

The penny-whistle player was on a window ledge, one leg inside and one outside, the cold coming in around his leg with indifference on both sides. He was perhaps forty, with the settled quality of a man who has found the position he wants and will occupy it until he decides otherwise. The guitar was across the room: a young woman in a green jersey, her head bent over the body of the instrument in the private absorption of someone working on a passage that was not finishing right. She tried the phrase again. It did not resolve. She shook her head, not with frustration but with the mild assessment of someone crossing a word off a list, and tried it once more at a slower pace.

Nobody introduced anyone. Miriam took a crate near the door. Ruth found a chair without a back that held her weight well enough, and for a while nothing was said that required a response and the street noise came up through the open window and the woman tried the passage again, and this time found the turn in it, and went through it twice at tempo and then laid her hand flat on the strings and let it be.

She raised her head.

'There's people going door to door on Hanover again,' she said. Her voice had the quality of a weather report: accurate, undramatised, addressed to the room in general. 'Two of them. Clipboards. One's a municipal shirt. I recognised him from March. Different face, same shirt.'

The man on the window ledge moved his chin a fraction. He looked at the street below. 'Different from the property survey lot?'

'Different people. Same purpose.' She tuned the low string a quarter turn. 'My sister puts the children's drawings on the wall. The teacher told her to. Makes the room feel like a home, the teacher said. She's had seventeen months of drawings up on that wall.' A pause. 'When they come to measure the room for the register, they'll tell her the drawings are not structural.' She picked a single open chord, letting it die. 'Not a permanent fixture. Not part of the property.'

The room absorbed this. The argument from the floor below surfaced briefly through the ceiling, the discussion of Joseph's theory carrying its own internal logic, and then receded.

'Boeta's building had a letter,' the penny-whistle player said. To the street below, not to the room. 'About the zoning category. Not the rent. The category.'

The woman with the guitar raised her hand from the strings and looked at her fingers for a moment, taking an inventory of them. She lowered her hand. She began the passage again, from the beginning, slowly.

---

Coming back down the building's stairs and through the courtyard, past the boy who had not looked up from his bicycle across the full duration of their visit, they emerged onto the lane. The sound opened: the lane's compressed echo giving way to the wider register of Caledon Street, a fishmonger's voice carrying two blocks in the wind, someone on a stoep above calling to someone on an adjacent building in the easy tone of a place where the distance between people was measured in feet rather than in convention.

Ruth walked and said nothing. A woman came out of a door on the left with a basin of washing and passed on the right with a cloth bag, and these two acknowledged each other with the minimal shared nod of people who have crossed in exactly this way on every Tuesday of their adult lives, a currency between them so familiar it required no counting. A man was reading a letter on his front step with the total attention of someone in possession of news they have been waiting for and are now alone with. The football argument from the lane above had migrated half a block and shifted partly back into Afrikaans for the next round of it.

Before they reached Caledon a man came out of the café at the corner and held the door behind him for an older woman in a hat coming out at the same time. She thanked him in Afrikaans. He answered her in what was not quite either language, four words that took the shortest path between Afrikaans and English and required no decision about which one to arrive in. Both of them were already going in their separate directions before the café door had finished closing.

---

Miriam's father's shop ran the length of a narrow room off Caledon Street, the shelving made to the ceiling on both walls, the top shelves running on a ladder-rail and occupied by stock that had been there long enough to simply belong there: tins with labels beginning to lift at the corners, a large jar with a hand-written label in a script she could not read, a row of bottles on their sides in the arrangement of something not recently moved. Below the point where regular use began, the stock was dense and ordered in a logic that had nothing to do with product category as a manufacturer would organise it. Things that belonged to the same kitchen stood together: tinned tomatoes alongside canned beans alongside dried chillies alongside the specific cumin she had been smelling all morning from doorways. Things used in the same season, the same meal, the same sequence of a working week. A Lindt tin near the front door with no chocolate in it held the money.

A woman came in for samp and stayed for twenty minutes. She and Miriam's father discussed the samp, a recent adjustment in the price of samp, a man called Joseph who had a theory about the adjusted price of samp, and the general reliability of Joseph's various theories over time. The conversation about Joseph widened naturally into an examination of his youngest daughter, who had recently taken a position at the hospital on De Waal Drive, and from there into a precise and unhurried analysis of what the hospital expected the young women who worked there to provide versus what it was prepared to publicly acknowledge that it expected. This last subject was concluded with the same efficiency as the samp.

Miriam's father looked at Ruth during the final part of this conversation. She kept her face still.

'Your people farm?' he said, when the woman had gone.

'Yes. Lowveld.'

'Hard country.'

'In the dry years.'

He looked at her in the same manner Jonas had, a private assessment against private criteria. Then he reached under the counter and produced a crate. 'Miriam's aunt has been waiting for this since Monday,' he said. 'Miriam knows the stairs.'

The crate was fabric bolts: lighter than its look suggested, requiring two people not for the weight but for the angles of the stairs. Ruth took the high end. Miriam took the low. They went through the shop's back door, past the washing yard, two lines strung taut and running sheets and shirts going stiff in the cold, and up the internal stairs cut at the steep angle of a building that did not intend to waste floor space.

Miriam's aunt was a woman of about fifty with the broad-shouldered authority of someone who has been the organising intelligence of a working household for long enough that the household has organised itself entirely around her method. She received the crate without performed gratitude. She pointed at the chairs below the window.

'Sit,' she said. 'There's tea coming.'

The room was the right size for what was in it: a cutting table kept clear of everything except the current work, bolts racked on the wall in the sequence of active use, a narrow kitchen partition fitted with complete domestic efficiency, everything at the height and proximity the work required. Through the window the side lane was visible, the cobbles drying unevenly in the last of the sun, and on the building opposite a row of chrysanthemums in clay pots along the balcony, orange and yellow, receiving the afternoon light at a practised angle.

The tea came in cups that were not matched and carried no apology for it. Ruth held hers in both hands. The aunt moved through the bolts from the crate, laying each against the table edge to read the quality of the cut end before refolding, the motions brisk and silent and entirely self-sufficient. Miriam had been assigned a section of the rack to resort and was doing it in a running exchange with the aunt about which bolts went where and why, the brown rather than the grey because the grey pulled light wrong when the material moved, the light blue for the woman on Hout Street whose pattern wanted the weight without the drape. Ruth followed enough of it to know it was a domestic professional language, shorthand built from a shared history of specific jobs with specific clients over a long time.

Outside, a child was bouncing a ball against the lane wall in the repeating rhythm of someone who has found exactly the wall and the afternoon they needed. The bounce came at steady intervals. When it skipped once the small irregularity resolved itself and the rhythm resumed.

Ruth finished the first cup. The aunt set a second in front of her without looking up from the bolt end she was checking, without asking, without noting the provision of it. The motion had the ease of a hand in its own kitchen, knowing what is needed and supplying it without requiring the transaction to be visible. Ruth picked it up.

'Your father's from up-country?' the aunt said, still on the fabric.

'Near Tzaneen. Lowveld.'

'Long way south.' She refolded the bolt with exact, practised creases. 'My husband's brother had the transport route to Duiwelskloof for a while before the war. He said the bushveld in a good rain was the most beautiful country he'd seen.'

'Yes,' Ruth said. 'It is.'

The aunt nodded as if this confirmed something she had privately believed, and returned to the end of the bolt.

Miriam moved to the far end of the table, where a second bolt was wanting a second pair of hands. She took the free end and pulled it taut. The aunt ran the tape the full length with a single motion and called the number before the tape was off the material, wrote it, and passed the bolt to the rack. Ruth stood. On the table a third bolt lay with its free end beginning to drift sideways in the draught from the window. She went to the table and took the end and held it square and steady at the corner, the cloth cool against her palms.

The aunt glanced at it. She said nothing. She ran the tape, noted the measurement, and moved to the next piece. Miriam brought a bolt from the crate and resorted it to its position on the wall. The work went on. Ruth passed the third bolt to the rack without being asked where it went, went back to the table, held the next free end when it began to drift.

A year and a half of accounting for herself on arrival, of working out the right register before speaking, of being the kind of useful that came with credentials attached. This room required only that her hands stay still. The aunt neither thanked her nor noted her presence.

Ruth looked at the chrysanthemums on the balcony opposite. The wind was worrying the outer petals, lifting and flattening them with each gust, and the pots were too heavy to move, the plants taking the wind as a condition and not a problem. She counted: she had been in this room for roughly forty minutes. She had not done that calculation until now. She had not calculated anything in that time.

---

They came back down to Hanover Street in the late afternoon, the south-easter still coming off the mountain, the pennants and signs all running flat to the east. The amber light of the late winter afternoon was working the stone fronts of the terraced houses, finding the ochre and cream beneath the grey wash and briefly making them distinct. The man on the ladder at the corner was finishing the orange lettering above the shop, going back over each letter before the paint set, small corrections against the wind's interference.

Ruth watched the letters for a moment. Beneath the orange, in the ghost-layer visible at the joins and curves, the previous name was still present: lighter, the colour of something subsiding, but there. The old Afrikaans name in the substrate of the wall, the new English name over it, and when the orange dried the old letters would be invisible from the pavement without being removed.

A notice board had been fixed to the wall outside Jonas's shop. A municipal backing frame, text in two parallel columns, English and Afrikaans. From the pavement Ruth could read enough of the Afrikaans to assemble the shape of it: *hersonering*, *register van eiendomme*, a date, a reference number. A man she had not seen before was standing close to the board, reading it. His hands were deep in his jacket pockets, his shoulders pulled fractionally inward in the way of someone weathering something that has come from outside. He finished, and stood still for a moment more without moving. Then he walked back toward Caledon Street at a pace that was ordinary and unhurried.

Jonas was closing his shop, the half-door drawn to with the routine finality of end-of-day. He saw Ruth looking at the board.

'Same sheet went up on four other streets last Tuesday,' he said. Not to Ruth exactly. To the space between them and the board. 'They put them all up on the same morning. Very organised. Same language, same date, same reference number.' He lifted his chin toward the board with the respect of someone acknowledging a well-played opposing move. 'They are organised. I'll give them that.'

He went inside. The pole had stopped turning. The shop lamp came on behind the half-door.

---

The tram came down from the upper routes at twenty past four, the overhead lines hissing in the damp air. Ruth and Miriam climbed aboard without discussion and found seats. Miriam pushed her hair off her forehead. Ruth looked at the window.

The city moved backward past the glass in the reverse of the morning's direction: first the terraced rows, then the Observatory with its dome catching the last of the amber light, then the lower suburbs beginning, the stone walls and oaks coming in as the tram rose and the district's roofline lowered and then disappeared behind the curve of the hill. Ruth could see it for a moment at the De Waal intersection, the full spread of it below the cloudbase: the dense mass of the rooflines, the water tanks and washing lines on the upper floors, the mountain behind it pressing down with the same proximity the watercolour in the stairwell had shown. Then the tram moved on.

The seats in the third-class section were wooden and the cold had been in them all day. Miriam had a notebook open on her knee and was reading something she had folded inside it. Ruth watched the last buildings of the lower suburb give way to the oaks and the stone walls of the university precinct, the architecture changing as the tram climbed: the compressed, layered density of the district's terraced streets giving way to the open spacing of the upper city, buildings set back from the road with their gardens showing, their cut stone a different argument in the same stone.

The branch line from De Waal took them past the lower campus wall and the last of the light went off the stone at the exact moment they passed the gate. Grey after amber, the buildings going formal and slightly severe in the even winter dusk.

Miriam said something about a seminar Thursday, the Fitzgerald being due back in the library. Ruth said yes. She looked at the window.

The upper city came in: the university gates, the oaks along the lower avenue, the residence block dark against the sky, its upper windows carrying yellow light. The tram bell rang and they were on the pavement.

---

They walked up through the oaks from the stop in what remained of the light. Ruth's shoes were damp from the lanes. The residence was ahead, the granite walls going from brown to near-black as the last light left them.

She stopped at the bottom of the steps without deciding to.

Miriam had gone three steps up and paused, looking back.

'I'll come back,' Ruth said. It was not addressed to Miriam exactly. It came out of the same part of herself as the comment to the barber, before the considered version had revised it.

'Tuesday afternoons are good,' Miriam said. 'The aunt works on Tuesdays.' She went up.

Ruth did not follow for a moment. The cold on this side of the building was a different cold from the lanes, moving air rather than held stone, and after a long day in enclosed spaces and narrow streets the openness of it felt slightly too simple, too unoccupied. She could hear the wind in the oaks above the path. Below the residence, below the avenue and the oaks and the university gates, the lower city was under its cloud and invisible.

She had not stopped at the tailor's shop on the return to the tram. They had passed the doorway and she had not slowed. The wind had been at her back and Miriam was ahead and she had kept walking. The tailor's door had been half closed by then, the cigarette gone from the ashtray, the man still at his table with the dark cloth and his head down.

The drawing was still on the wall above his workbench. The house in blue crayon. The sun with the careful, even lines going out from it in every direction, the sun itself placed very close to the roof of the house in the composition, lower than a sun should sit in a picture of a house, the radiating lines nearly reaching the eave. Not crushing it. Closer in the drawing than a sun would be allowed to be and the house still standing under it, the lines going straight out to the edges of the paper.

A child had made it. Six, seven years old at the most, going by the pressure of the pencil lines on the blue crayon, the way the roof ridge was not quite level, the chimney added with a second decision and a slightly different blue. Done with the concentrated care of someone for whom the house mattered. Not a sketch. A record.

She had not stopped. Some things about the drawing had become clear on the walk to the tram and she had not said them to Miriam and she did not say them now.

She climbed the steps and went inside.

Chapter 9: The Price of Ease

The fourth time Ruth sat at the Cohen table, she reached for the water carafe without thinking.

That was the specific thing: the absence of the thinking. The carafe stood where it had stood on both previous evenings, between the nearer candlestick and the bread plate, the glass heavy and slightly fogged at the base where the kitchen produced its particular condensation. Her hand found it. Her hand poured, set the carafe down, and the thing was done before she had registered doing it, and the evening continued.

Mr Cohen was asking David something about the meeting at the university hall. It moved mid-sentence into something about an architect's registration, or perhaps they were the same person. Ruth had not caught the beginning. David's hands were already off his fork, resting knuckled on the tablecloth. He ate quickly and then became still, attentive, the instrument-ready quality he had, as though the real activity were still ahead. 'The ones who speak loudest about conditions,' he said, 'have never sat in the same room as anyone whose conditions they are speaking about. They argue at a high theoretical volume and the working man is in their kitchen.'

'Not in front of company,' said Mrs Cohen. Her voice was its own instrument, dry and precise, with the economy of a woman who did not repeat herself.

'Ruth isn't company,' said Judith on Ruth's left, without looking up from the bread she was tearing. She said it the way she said most things: as a factual statement of the current situation, delivered without inflection.

Mrs Cohen made a short sound, not agreement and not refusal entirely. She lifted her wine.

'She isn't,' Mr Cohen said. Large hands around his glass, calm and measured, the sort of man who settled small questions by stating what was obviously the case and then attending to his meal. He did not look at Ruth as he said it, which was its own form of inclusion. 'Which is why she's permitted to hear it.'

David looked at Ruth once, the brief evaluating glance he had, and smiled. 'What Ruth actually thinks is the most interesting opinion at this table,' he said, 'but she won't say it in front of my mother.'

Ruth held her glass and looked at the candle flame, which was still in the closed apartment air. She said nothing, not because the remark required defence but because her silence had for some weeks ceased to register with the table as reticence. They had filed it as Ruth's tempo, the particular pace at which she moved between hearing and answering, and they had adjusted to it, and the adjustment was comfortable in both directions.

She had blushed here once, badly. The first visit: the neck and the tops of the ears, the dining-room light designed apparently to find those surfaces. She had been conscious of it and conscious simultaneously that awareness of the blush made the blush worse, which was a problem without a solution. It had not recurred at the second visit, not badly. Tonight it was simply not there.

She picked up a piece of bread because she wanted it. The basket was where it had been.

The apartment itself was familiar now in the way places become familiar before you can explain how: she knew the smell of the lobby, brass polish and floor wax and the particular damp warmth of the lift shaft, knew which stair tread gave slightly under weight, knew the angle at which the corridor from the entrance to the dining room received the last of the evening light off the sea. She knew Mr Cohen's chair, the one with the worn arm where his right hand rested. She knew the kitchen sounds from behind the half-open door, when they meant a stage of preparation and when they meant recovery. She knew all of this without having counted it, and the knowing-without-counting was itself the thing she had noted tonight when her hand found the carafe.

Sarah came through from the kitchen with both hands around the handles of the brisket platter, set it at the centre of the table with a practised and careful quiet. The black cotton dress under the stiff white apron. The face lined deep around the mouth. Eyes that met no one at the table but whose positioning was absolutely exact: the bread plate, the spare spoon, the space between the candle and the serving ladle, all of it located without a look. She set the platter down and stepped back, and the room reorganised itself around the brisket, as rooms do around the arrival of the thing they have been moving towards all evening.

Mrs Cohen served. Judith first, then Ruth, then David, then Mr Cohen, then herself. An order said something: preference, seniority, some hierarchy of claim worked out over years. Ruth had been trying to read it and had not yet managed a complete reading.

'You corrected Professor Hendricks on Thursday,' said Judith to Ruth, turning the gravy spoon. 'In the tutorial. You told him it was the genitive.'

'It was.'

'He knew it was.'

'Then he didn't mind.'

Judith laughed, a low clean sound, no performance in it. 'She's impossible,' she said to the room.

'Accurate,' said Mr Cohen, without raising his eyes from his plate. One word of precise endorsement and then the topic entirely released, as though he had signed a small document and placed it on the completed pile. Ruth had learned not to expect expansion and had come to find the brevity useful. In an intelligent person, brevity was a reliable signal of what required no further argument.

David was looking at the middle distance in the way he sometimes did, a private attending. He had the quality, when not speaking, of hearing something the room had not yet produced. 'Book four,' he said to Ruth. 'The Odyssey.'

'Yes.'

He nodded without continuing, but the interest was real, and he reached for the bread in the way of a person still thinking about something else.

The brisket was the same: onion and fat and the long sweetness of the slow braise. The candles were past their halfway point. Outside, Friday on the seafront road, the tram grinding its electric note around the lower curve towards Sea Point proper. In the kitchen, the brief sound of it, the wire brush on the iron pan, Sarah beginning the pots while the main course was still on the table, the kitchen on its own clock and its own accounting, moving parallel to the dinner and not waiting for it.

Ruth ate and said little, and the conversation went as it went: Mr Cohen with the newspaper folded on the vacant side of the table, David with the students at the university hall, Mrs Cohen with three or four precise social observations that functioned less as information than as calibration, a weekly statement of what the world was still correctly positioned to be. And in all of it, she was watching herself from a slight distance. The balance of her own body. Not checking the exits. Not measuring the candlelight for exposure. Not converting the silver into debt arithmetic in the way she had still been doing in February, when every displayed object in the room had required translation into what it cost her.

She reached for the carafe a second time because Judith's glass was low and the carafe was already in her hand, and poured without comment. The act was simpler than setting it down first.

After dessert, after the brandy David did not take and Mr Cohen did, after David had risen without announcement and gone to the piano in the sitting room as he always did, somewhere between nine and nine-thirty, which was never announced and never commented on by anyone in the family, after Mrs Cohen had excused herself in the direction of the kitchen, the two of them were left at the cleared but not yet wiped table. Two plates still with their forks placed diagonally. One candlestick had burned through and the wick sat in a small pool of hardened wax that had run and cooled against the base.

'You're quieter than usual,' said Judith.

'I'm thinking about the third declension.'

Judith received this in silence, which was correct. She had learned by now that Ruth's thinking was a separate country that continued operating at a low and continuous pitch during social hours, and that this was not distance but constitution.

From the sitting room the piano. David's habit was fragments, not full pieces. He played the way some people talk to themselves: in running thoughts, half-finished, turning back. Three descending chords. A pause. A long run in the upper register, falling, something he was finding rather than stating. Something in the bass, exploratory and then abandoned.

'Is he well?' Ruth said.

Judith looked at the spent candlewick. 'He's David,' she said. Not an answer, but said in the tone of a woman who had received the same non-answer for twenty years and had long since incorporated it.

The piano went still.

Sarah came back through to clear the remaining plates. She gathered them with the efficient and essentially invisible care of a person who had made the work disappear as a professional matter. She did not look at Ruth tonight. She looked at the tablecloth as she sometimes did: past its surface, to some middle distance the dining room did not contain. Her left hand was lower and slightly turned as she stacked, distributing the weight of the gathered plates, and Ruth watched the hand without deciding to watch it, noticing the gradual turn and the stack and the practised management of load, and the kitchen sounds beginning again through the half-open door almost before Sarah had cleared the threshold.

Ruth took the tram back alone, as she had at each visit. The offer of a cab had come on the first visit, been declined, and had not been repeated, which was one of the things about Judith: she offered once and then released the outcome, with no further investment in which way it went.

The tram was quiet. A man at the front in a damp mackintosh, newspaper folded to the middle pages. Two women with bags in their laps and a sleeping child between them, the child's head against the window glass. A young man, probably a student, with a textbook open at a page he had not been reading for some time. The lights along the seafront road came and went in the window glass, amber and white, and the occasional red of a motor car heading north.

Ruth sat with her hands in her lap and the coat buttoned to the collar. The mountain somewhere behind the window to her left, invisible in the dark but making itself felt in the temperature, the cold of the rock coming off the wind even through the tram glass. She was not calculating anything in particular. She was watching the sleeping child's profile, the newspaper-reader's thumb at the fold, the student's untouched page. She was there, or as close to simply there as she generally managed, which was closer than in February by a specific measure she could not name but could register.

She counted the stops. Not because she risked missing hers but because it occupied the hands' attention while the other kind of attending continued.

There was an apple from the residence kitchen on her desk, and a note she had started in the margin of the day's passage: a genitive plural she had marked and wanted to confirm. She ate half the apple and made the note and reached for the apple again.

The smell arrived without being summoned. Not the dinner smell, not the brisket in its onion gravy or the candlewax or the warm gathered bread of the dining room. The other one. The pan: iron and the wire brush's darker bristle, the fat at the edge, the steam that came off the first water when it hit a still-hot surface. She had not been in the Cohen kitchen that evening. She had heard the brush sound through the half-open door, had not seen the pan, but the mind had been given enough of the components.

She sat with the half-eaten apple and the two smells, and they did not resolve into a conclusion. They existed alongside each other, the dining-room smell and the kitchen smell, two things true at once in the same body, and she sat with it.

She set the apple down.

She did not pick it up.

She looked at her father's letter. It had been on the corner of the desk since Wednesday. She had read it when it arrived: once quickly, once slowly. He wrote in his brief particular way. One paragraph for the farm news, which was the news of the accounts and the rainfall and the fence line on the lower paddock that would need re-straining before the dry season. One paragraph for Anna, which this time contained a sentence she had read twice: *the doctor is satisfied with the chest, which has not worsened since autumn, and she asks for you regularly.* One paragraph for the closing, which he always handled as a technical matter requiring resolution: keep well, write when you are able. Signed W.J. MacLeod in the full formal version of his name he used for anything written on paper.

She had drafted one paragraph of a reply and stopped. The paragraph said: she was well, the Greek was going better than last term, she had been to the Cohen household again and they were kind. Every word of it was true. The paragraph was not the letter. She had known this and had left it on the desk beside his letter for four days.

William had written in January. She had replied: two lines about the scholarship and the weather, both true. She had sealed the envelope before she read them back.

She picked up the pen. She wrote *Dear Dad* on a fresh page, and the date in the upper right corner, and then she put the pen down.

Outside, the mountain wind. Rain somewhere towards Thursday at the edge of it, the smell of eucalyptus and cold rock, not the subtropical mist-belt rain she had grown up inside, which was softer and warmer at the edges, greenish when it hit the red soil. This rain was the Atlantic kind. It found the gaps in stone.

She opened the Greek grammar to Tuesday's passage, read the first three lines, and went to bed.

She found Miriam in the small kitchen at the end of the corridor the following Saturday evening. Miriam had her head inside the bread tin, conducting the methodical end-of-week excavation that turned up, generally, three heels of different loaves, a packet of unclaimed Marie biscuits, and the occasional rind of hard yellow cheese that appeared with regularity and was never owned. Ruth wanted tea. She filled the kettle and set it on the ring.

'How was Sea Point?' said Miriam, still with her head in the tin.

'Fine.'

'Third time?'

'Fourth.'

Miriam surfaced with a packet of Marie biscuits and the expression of someone who had accounted for the situation and made the necessary adjustment. She sat on the counter, which was not permitted, and opened the packet. She held it out. Ruth shook her head.

Miriam ate the first biscuit without ceremony, looking at the wall above the kettle. She had that quality: thinking was visible in her face as a specific stillness, deep and local, before it produced a conclusion or produced nothing. A second biscuit with the same unhurried attention.

'They feed you properly over there,' she said. Not a question.

'Yes,' said Ruth.

'Good brisket?'

'Yes.'

'Good,' said Miriam, and looked at the wall, and it appeared to settle the matter of the Cohen household to her current satisfaction.

The kettle was building its sound. The steam began between them. Ruth held the mug and waited.

Miriam looked at her. Not a quick look, a proper one, the direct and unblinking attention she applied to things she had decided required it. Not intrusive and not unkind. The look of a person whose conclusions were worth the cost of their directness.

'You went in those shoes again,' Miriam said, looking at Ruth's feet.

'They're my only good shoes.'

'I know,' said Miriam. She ate a third biscuit and looked at the wall.

The kettle reached its temperature and clicked off. Ruth poured. Steam went up between them. Ruth wrapped both hands around the mug.

'You're getting fluent,' Miriam said.

Ruth looked at her. 'In what?'

The sideways glance, brief, landing on nothing new. 'In everything,' Miriam said. Then she folded the biscuit packet closed and laid it on the counter beside her, and the sentence was done, and she was not going to expand it.

Ruth took her tea.

In the corridor, walking back with the mug warm between both palms, she turned the word over. Fluent. Not *good* or *settled* or *easier*, but *fluent*: a word that contained a language, and a language implied a prior one, the language you spoke at home, the one you had before you acquired the other. You became fluent when you were operating in something that was not your native register. Fluent in everything was an observation about more than one thing at once: the Sea Point dining room, the Hanover Street afternoon last week with the sign-painter and the accordion women and Miriam pointing out the ghost letters beneath the orange paint, and possibly also the residence dining hall with Professor Hendricks and the genitive that was the genitive. Miriam had been near enough to all of those occasions to have the data, and she had noted the data and produced the word. The word was not accusation. It was measurement. The measurement was probably accurate.

The bracket in the corridor was humming. It had been humming for three weeks and the duty warden had twice promised to see to it.

Ruth went into her room and sat on the edge of the bed and did not drink the tea immediately. She sat, and the bracket hummed in the corridor, and she sat with it.

After a while she put the tea on the desk and worked on the Greek until the residence was quiet.

On Tuesdays their lecture schedules aligned at half-past three and they fell into the walk without planning it, the way regular appearances become habits before they become decisions. Down the oak line, the lower campus, the old service gate at the foot. The oaks were coming off their leaves early. The warden said it was the mountain wind stripping them more aggressively than a normal summer-end, but Ruth had no previous summer here to use as a comparison.

Judith had come from the economics subsidiary and was carrying something in her quiet that was different from the usual gathered ease. Her usual was settled and untroubled. This one had an edge to it, along the jaw.

They walked. The leaves off the oaks were dry at the edges, already starting to curl and tighten.

'He played all night again,' Judith said. 'Friday. After you left. Didn't stop until the light changed. My mother gets up around three and closes his door very quietly, as though that addresses it. But you can hear it through all the walls.'

Ruth said nothing. The leaves moved ahead of their own walking in the intermittent wind, always one step forward.

'When he can't leave,' Judith said, 'he plays. That's how it works for him. He plays in the dark until it's light and then he sleeps through the middle of the day and then he has breakfast and is perfectly available and polite, and we don't discuss any of it because my mother has decided it doesn't require discussion. It requires management.'

'Where does he want to go?' Ruth said.

Judith gave the question its due time. She always did this with direct questions, gave them their proper weight before answering. 'Palestine at the moment,' she said. 'Last spring it was Paris. My mother won't hear Palestine. She heard it once and closed the idea completely, like a cupboard door. My father hears it and decides not to have heard it, which is a different method with the same result. So the wanting has nowhere to go except the piano.' She kicked a small cluster of leaves off the path. 'He won't go. He'll stay and want to leave and be clever and play in the dark and they'll all love each other too much to cost each other anything, and that's the arrangement.'

She said this without cruelty and without self-pity. She said it the way she reported facts she had fully incorporated.

'Could he go?' Ruth said. 'Practically. The money.'

'Of course. It isn't the money.' Judith looked at the oak canopy above them, the mountain visible through a break in the leaves, upper rock grey with cloud. 'Palestine is what he says when he wants to ask permission. Paris is what he means when he doesn't expect it. Either way he stays. The arrangement holds because it holds itself. Nobody breaks an arrangement that reflects back only love.'

They walked in quiet.

Ruth said: 'My father came to the station. When I left from Pietersburg. He drove in from the farm. He didn't have to come. It wasn't practical, it wasn't on his way anywhere. He stood on the platform and he said the practical things and then the train went.'

Judith looked at her.

'My mother didn't come,' Ruth said. 'She stayed at the farm.'

Judith said nothing. She did not say she was sorry and she did not ask about the mother and she did not fill the space with anything, and all three of those absences were correct.

After a moment Judith said, quietly: 'I think that's probably where it comes from. In you. The exactness. People who've been left at platforms get very precise about what they can and can't reasonably carry.'

Ruth did not answer. She turned the idea at a slight angle, the way she turned a grammar construction she was not yet sure of, suspicious of anything too complete. It was either entirely right or entirely wrong. It was probably right and wrong in the same breath, in the way that things said about you which you cannot immediately dismiss are usually at least partly right.

They reached the lower gate. The wind stronger here, direct from the mountain, colder, salt-mixed. Ruth buttoned the top button of her coat. Her hand went into the right pocket for the warmth of it and found the silk square, folded where she had last set it, weeks ago now.

She registered the awareness. Nothing more than that. Still there.

Judith glanced at the pocket. Not a long look, brief, precise, the duration of a look that says *I see that* and then moves on. She looked back at the path.

She did not ask.

Ruth did not offer.

The gate opened. They walked out onto the lower road. The oak trees behind them, the mountain above and the city below, and the leaves from the tree at the gate corner came off in the gust and moved across the road in no organised direction, east and then west as the next wind came off the rock.

They walked towards the tram stop. Judith talked about the economics paper she had not finished, and Ruth listened, and the afternoon light came in low and lateral off the bay as it did at the close of summer, flattening the white buildings slightly, deepening the shadows under the mountain.

Past ten, the corridor was quiet. The humming bracket had been repaired sometime in the last two days. Its absence was a brief disorientation, the way any removed sound draws the ear until the ear adjusts and the silence becomes ordinary.

Ruth sat at her desk with the Greek grammar open to book four. The single lamp. The mountain wind at the window frame. Somewhere below on the road a tram going south, its electric note brief and then absorbed.

The passage was book four: Telemachus in Sparta, in the palace of Menelaus, the gold and amber of the great hall, the youth's awe at wealth he had never seen, the whole household organised around a king who fought a war for ten years and came home. Ruth had read it twice in preparation. She read the first hexameter aloud in an undertone in the practised manner, measuring the quantities, the line building in its movement to the sixth foot.

She read it.

She closed the grammar.

The wind measured itself against the window glass. A second tram on the lower road, going south. Then nothing.

She reached behind her and lifted the coat from the chair-back and reached into the right pocket and removed the silk square. She unfolded it once along its crease. The emerald sat in the lamplight with the quality silk has in low light, a density that did not require brightness, something in the weave that held the colour even in a dim room. She brought it close and the smell came: lavender, cedar, the expensive-shop smell that had no single identifiable constituent but was exactly recognisable as the combined evidence of a whole way of living, the kind in which lavender was a real plant grown in a real garden, and cedar was a real wood in a real clothes press, and the shop had been in the same position on the same street for thirty years and would be there thirty years hence without any particular uncertainty about whether it would be asked to move.

She held it for a moment.

She folded the square once along its crease and put it back in the coat pocket.

She turned to the grammar and opened it to the beginning of the passage and read the first hexameter again, quietly, in the room.

She did not turn the page.

Chapter 10: The British House

The invitation had been on the desk for five days before Ruth allowed herself to read it as something that required an answer.

The notebook was beside it, closed. She had not opened it since Friday.

It sat beside the Greek grammar and the scholarship folder, a cream envelope, the handwriting firm and forward-slanting, the kind formed by years of girls' school penmanship instruction. She had opened it on the Tuesday and read it once and set it down again without committing to anything. She had read it again on Wednesday evening after tutorial, when the lamp was on and the room was quiet and the mountain was pressing close against the window glass. The card inside was thick and slightly scented, nothing she could name precisely, something floral and dry, and the words on it ran in a clean, authoritative hand: Mrs Audrey Lawrence and Colonel Thomas Lawrence request the pleasure of Miss MacLeod's company at Bellevue, Oranjezicht, Saturday 12th March, seven o'clock. At the bottom, in a slightly different ink: Your father's cousin sends her warmest regards. We have heard wonderful things from William. We hope you will come.

Now it was Saturday and the light outside was already failing towards five.

Ruth stood before the spotted mirror on the wardrobe door and adjusted her collar for what she estimated was the ninth or tenth time. The glass was old, its silvering failing at the edges so that the outermost inch of her reflection dissolved into the cream-painted wall behind her. She looked at herself in the centre of what remained. The dress was the better of the two she owned, dark wool, cut square at the shoulder, worn to a slight shine along the left hip where she had been pressing the scholarship folder against her body for months. She had pressed the dress the previous night with a damp cloth and the residence's communal iron. The seam along the right sleeve still held a ghost of a pucker she had not managed to smooth out, and she had looked at it for a long time before deciding it would only be visible in certain light.

She fastened the top button. She unfastened it again.

The room's winter cold was in the fabric, in the floor, in the stone sill of the window where the condensation had sat all morning. It was in her, in the particular set of her shoulders, in the way she kept glancing at the card on the desk and then looking deliberately away from it. She had known this posture since the convent. It was the posture of a girl standing outside a room in which assessment was already in progress.

She picked up Judith's silk square from the desk. She had been carrying it since the previous Friday, folded once in her coat pocket, retrieved each morning with the intention of returning it, each evening replaced in the pocket untouched. She pressed it between her fingers now. It was pale green and silk-smooth, carrying the last of the Cohen apartment: beeswax, lavender, and the particular warm air of a house where arguments happened over good wine and no one was required to perform composure. She knotted it at her throat. It pulled the dress together and gave the collar something to settle against.

She looked at her hands. The nails were short and clean. The skin below the second knuckle of the right index finger still held a dark half-moon of library ink that three days of the residence's harsh yellow soap had not fully lifted. Her hands were the hands she had always had. The nails of a girl who worked. The ink of a girl who read. She looked at them for a moment and put them at her sides.

Through the window the mountain was already losing its definition, the upper rock face fading from the mottled grey-brown of daylight into the undifferentiated dark of evening. The south-easter was making its rounds, a long unvarying note in the eaves, the pines beyond the playing fields moving in slow collective agreement. The city below the slopes was turning its lights on one by one, small, warm assertions against the large cold of the night.

The scholarship folder lay on the desk. She did not open it.

She put on her coat and went to the door.

The corridor outside Room 412 was quiet. Saturday had emptied the residence in stages: the socials first, trailing cedar and laughter by five o'clock, gone by half past with their weekend ease; then the day-students; then the ordinary working evenings of the scholarship girls behind their doors. Behind one door, a typewriter clicking with steady concentration. Behind another, a radio barely audible, a voice reading something in Afrikaans, the words too muffled to distinguish. Ruth walked the length of the corridor to the high window at the far end and looked out at the ocean.

The Atlantic at this hour was a flat hammered grey, already losing its light from the west, the surface smooth in the lull between the day-wind and the night-wind. She stood for a moment with her forehead not quite touching the glass, the cold of it radiating into the few inches of air between them. Then she went down the stairs.

She had telephoned the taxi firm that afternoon from the residence payphone, listening to the Bakelite click of the dial and then the distant ring of a line reaching across the city. The man who answered quoted her a price for Oranjezicht without asking how she proposed to pay it. She had said yes and given him the address and then stood in the phone box for a moment after the receiver was down, listening to the street.

She waited at the kerb in the cold. The pines along the residence path moved above her with a dry, rhythmic sound, needle-heavy and carried on the evening wind. Somewhere down the hill, a tram was working its way up from Observatory, its overhead lines giving their high harmonic against the dark. She stood with her coat buttoned and her small clutch bag held in both hands and watched the road.

The taxi appeared at the bottom of the hill, one headlamp working, the engine a low complaint that grew closer and then cut as the driver pulled to the kerb. A black car with a dented near-side fender. The driver was a middle-aged man in a flat cap, a cigarette burning in the corner of his mouth, who did not offer to take her bag. He put the car in second before she had the door fully closed.

'Oranjezicht,' Ruth said. 'The Lawrence house. Bellevue, upper Hof Street.'

He nodded without looking at her and pulled out.

The car moved through streets that changed register with each block. Below the university's stone walls and pines, Observatory arrived: terraced houses pressed close, iron balconies with washing lines running between them, the week's sheets still out in the night air, two boys kicking a tin can along the pavement without looking up as the taxi passed. The houses grew wider apart as the road climbed. The trees changed from the straggling municipal planes of the lower slopes to the planted species of the upper suburbs, oaks and a copper beech heavy and dark behind one wall, and the dense, dark laurels that the English families had brought from home a century before and that had grown, in the Cape climate, to a size that nothing in England ever grew to.

Through the vibrating glass Ruth watched the lights of the lower city fall away on her left. The docks first, the dark shapes of the freight vessels under the arc lights with the small, moving figures of the longshoremen against the white. Then the terraced streets of the District, the iron balconies and the lamp-glow in individual windows and the dense concentration of lives arranged to use every square foot of available space. Then the flat rooftops of the lower suburbs giving way, the geometry loosening. The Atlantic was briefly visible between two buildings, wide and very dark, no light on it, only the idea of open water, and then the buildings closed again and they were on the upper roads and the city was below them.

The houses up here set back behind walls. The gardens Ruth glimpsed above the wall tops were green and attended, the hedges cut recently, the pale ends of the trimmed branches showing clean in the lamplight. The laurels along the street verge were enormous, glossy and utterly still, as if the wind above them moved by arrangement and the laurels had declined to participate. A dog barked behind one of the walls and fell silent immediately. The street smelled of wet stone and pine resin and, below that, very faintly, wood smoke from a fireplace somewhere in the houses above.

Ruth held her small bag in her lap. The card was inside it. She did not open the bag.

The taxi stopped before a pair of gates painted a deep, formal black. They were tall and slightly ornate at the top, the iron worked into acanthus leaves with a gilded finial on each gate, the gold remaining in patches where the Cape weather had not yet stripped it. The gates stood open. A gravel drive curved away through the lower garden and rose towards the house at the far end, visible through the dark: double-storey, Victorian in construction, with deep verandas on both levels and the sitting-room lights burning warm behind glass. Two other cars were parked along the verge near the gate posts.

The driver put on the handbrake.

Ruth counted coins and handed them across. His hand was large and calloused, the knuckles pale in the car's interior light. He closed his fist around the fare without looking at it and without looking at her. The car reversed, turned, and descended the slope between the laurels, its tail-lights two small red points in the dark of the street until the laurels closed and they were gone.

She was alone at the gates. The open ironwork stood on either side of her, present and formal, the hinges massive and oiled and held wide. The house at the top of the drive was lit and the gramophone inside it was audible from the kerb, thin and domestic, reduced to a thread of orchestral music by distance and cold air. The mountain was directly above the house, invisible in the dark, but the wind that came down off the rock face was cold and mineral and carried the smell of the stone.

She adjusted the silk square at her throat and walked up the drive.

The garden on either side was old and heavy, the roses cut back for winter into a collection of careful, architectural stumps, a fig tree in the lower corner trained against the wall the way the convent trees had been trained, its branches spread flat and tied, domesticated out of any shape that resembled its wild self. Her boots made a carrying sound on the raked gravel with each step. The veranda steps were flagstone, worn smooth in the centre. Red geraniums in pots on either side of the front door, still holding their colour under the lamp. The door was painted dark green, its brass fittings polished to a deep reflective shine, slightly distorting her shape as she came up the last of the steps.

Before she could reach for the bell, the door opened.

The woman who opened it was Cape Coloured, somewhere in her fifties, wearing a grey cotton dress under a soft white apron with no starch in it, the fabric loose and long-washed. Her hair was pulled back under a white cloth. She had the calm, settled manner of a person who had been answering this door for years and had long since stopped treating it as remarkable.

'Miss MacLeod?' she said.

'Yes.' Ruth was briefly surprised and then was not.

'Come in. Mrs Lawrence is in the drawing room.' A pause, quiet and deliberate. 'May I take your coat?'

Ruth gave her the coat. The woman folded it once over her arm with careful economy, the movement exact, the effort precisely calculated for the task. Ruth watched her do it and thought, without meaning to think it, of Sarah's hands closing around the wax-paper parcel of brisket in the Cohens' kitchen four weeks ago, the same quality of practised weight in the wrists. The thought arrived without announcement and left the same way. She followed the woman into the hall.

The hall was warm and still in a way the outside was not, smelling of cut flowers and beeswax, the coal fire drawing steadily in the adjacent room. Dark oak boards under an oriental carpet, deep red and blue, worn pale in a track down its centre where the generations had walked. A barometer in a mahogany case on the wall above the coat stand. A framed survey map of the Cape Province, hand-coloured, with the old Dutch names for the mountain ranges legible in small brown script. Beside the drawing-room door, a brass umbrella stand with two walking sticks and a furled umbrella.

The drawing-room doors were open. Ruth heard the voices before she entered: three conversations running, the gramophone playing Brahms, the fire's crack, a woman's laughter cut short. She stood for a moment in the doorway.

The room was large and furnished with things that had been very good when new and had held their character through decades of use. Two sofas and a scatter of chairs arranged around the fireplace. A writing table by the far window. A drinks cabinet in the corner with the decanters set out and already in use. The bookshelves ran floor to ceiling on the wall beside the door, their contents a mixture of proper books, a complete set of something in burgundy bindings, some volumes shelved horizontal for want of attention, and on the top shelf a series of objects: a naval sextant in its wooden case, a framed photograph of a ship at anchor in pre-war harbour light, a small bronze horse with one foreleg raised.

Nine people. Ruth counted without appearing to count.

Mrs Lawrence was on the nearest sofa with her back to the door, a narrow woman in dark blue, grey hair set and lacquered. She was talking to a man who stood with his drink held at waist height and his tie at the second button, laughing at something she had said. Two younger men by the drinks cabinet, both with the short-back-and-sides of men who had done service recently enough to still be performing it, one in a naval uniform. A couple by the fire, both fiftyish, the woman turning a book spine in her hands. A young woman alone near the windows, whose particular thinness and stillness set her apart from the room's general movement.

And in the corner chair, separated from the others by a clear two feet of unoccupied carpet, a man Ruth identified as Thomas Lawrence.

He was not old. Forty-something, thin in a way the body did not choose so much as arrive at, his hands wrapped around a glass of water rather than the whisky the others were drinking. His face had the quality of a face that is keeping itself very still. The jaw set, the gaze fixed on a particular point in the fire above the grate, the stillness slightly off in the way that a painting of a person is slightly off from a person: too controlled to be natural, too sustained to suggest comfort. His uniform was an old one, cleaned and pressed, as if he had put it on and then not changed again. He did not look up when Ruth came in.

Mrs Lawrence rose with a quick, efficient movement. Her handshake was firm and dry. Once, released.

'Miss MacLeod. I am so pleased you came.' The conviction behind it was practised but genuine, the two things not in contradiction. She looked at Ruth with the same accuracy that the letter had promised. Her gaze went briefly to the silk square and back. 'William wrote about you, you know. He is not a natural correspondent. He writes rarely and says even less in what he writes. But he wrote about you.' She tilted her head in the direction of the drinks cabinet. 'What will you drink? The sherry is a Manzanilla and quite decent, if sherry agrees with you. The man in the naval uniform is Pearce and he is better company than he appears.'

The man in question turned at the sound of his name and said that he had heard much worse, and poured the sherry without being asked and handed it to Ruth with the direct, easy manner of a man accustomed to rooms like this one and not unduly troubled by them. He had been at Tobruk and was now seconded to Simon's Town, he told her, and was extraordinarily dull, which she should know from the start, and she laughed before she could arrange her face against it.

The couple at the fire were the Rawlings, he a King's Counsel at the Cape Bar and she the daughter of a Port Elizabeth clergyman who had read Classics at Stellenbosch. She observed Ruth with a precise, contained interest when the scholarship was mentioned.

'Latin and Greek,' Mrs Rawlings said. 'You'll be in Arbuthnot's lectures.'

'When he's not reading us Agamemnon.'

'He is always reading someone Agamemnon,' Mrs Rawlings said. She held her drink and looked at the fire for a moment. 'The question he never asks is what Clytemnestra was doing for the ten years her husband was at Troy. The women in those plays have always done their suffering offstage. Arbuthnot calls that a structural feature.'

She said this last thing into the fire rather than to Ruth directly, but Ruth heard it. She filed it carefully in the place where she kept things she had not yet understood.

The man in the grey suit was Morrison, in land surveying. He had a voice that filled space without requiring attention, the satisfied, steady sound of a man for whom work was the primary moral category. The suit itself was better than the man, which she noted and then did not know what to do with. He talked about permit complications arising from the proposed relocations in the District and the lower suburbs, the logistical burden on his firm, and he used the word unfortunate three times in six sentences and appeared not to notice.

A young man in a white jacket moved through the room with the dishes. Ruth looked at him more carefully after the first glance and revised her initial impression: he was not young, forty at least, the whiteness of the jacket making the age harder to read. He moved with a practised absence of presence, offering the smoked salmon on its silver tray and the small pastry rectangles on their cloth, and the room absorbed his movement without acknowledging it. The conversations continued at their same volumes. The ring of glasses against each other was unchanged as he passed. He reached Mrs Rawlings. She took a pastry without looking at his hand and continued speaking. He reached Morrison. He reached Pearce.

He reached Ruth.

She looked directly at his face. He met her eyes for a fraction of a second, the expression nothing that she could name: not resentment, not subservience, not the bright professional warmth of someone who needs the gratuity. Simply the face of a person doing a task, making an assessment, reserving everything else. She took a pastry. He moved on. She watched him cross to the drinks cabinet, set the tray down, and pick up the second tray with the same measured rotation of purpose, his back to the room.

Across the room, in his corner chair, Thomas Lawrence was watching her.

She did not look away immediately. He did not look away either. He had the gaze of a man who had stopped caring about the social cost of looking directly at another person. Not rudeness. Something that had come after rudeness was no longer a consideration. After a moment the door from the dining room opened and something in the room shifted and Thomas Lawrence returned to his fire.

Dinner was laid in the adjacent room, set for ten and smelling of roast lamb and the beeswax-and-polish atmosphere of a large room kept carefully. Ruth was seated between Pearce and the young woman from the windows, who turned out to be Gillian, Thomas Lawrence's daughter from his first marriage, twenty-two years old, reading law.

'My father asked me to say he was sorry he hadn't spoken to you earlier,' Gillian said, quietly, serving herself potatoes without looking at Ruth.

'I didn't mind,' Ruth said.

Gillian set the dish down. A small, deliberate thing moved across her face. 'Most people want an explanation. He doesn't usually mention it.'

The conversation at the table ran into politics the way conversation in the city always ran into politics that winter, the same current beneath every other subject. Morrison spoke about the Group Areas planning as a logistical matter, the complications arising for his firm from the proposed removals, the resource allocation, the permit backlog. He spoke of it as a problem of scheduling, and the word unfortunate did its work again.

Rawlings said, after some time, that the government was proving very good at systems. He said 'the current government' in the tone one uses for weather: observational, at a remove, neither endorsement nor opposition and not quite neutral either. 'Efficient and cruel,' he added. 'Those two things are not in tension for them. They have made them the same thing.'

Mrs Lawrence looked down the table. 'Thomas was with men who had that view of organisation,' she said. 'Efficient and rational. They were building supply roads in the desert that lasted ten years.' She picked up her wine glass. 'The roads are still there.'

Nobody said anything for a moment. The young man in the white jacket moved around the table with the wine. He reached Morrison and refilled the glass and Morrison did not look up. The sentence continued uninterrupted. The young man moved behind Rawlings, behind the woman in the green dress, behind Mrs Lawrence. The table's conversations ran on at their same consistent level, the movement of the wine around the table as absorbed and unremarked as the ticking of the clock in the hall.

Ruth looked at her lamb. She thought of Sarah's hands on the wire brush at the Cohens' kitchen sink, the grey fat flying against the white tiles, the rhythm of the scrubbing. She thought of what Sarah had said about Hanover Street. She took a mouthful of the lamb and tasted the rosemary and the fat and the attention that had gone into the cooking of it and thought of who had cooked it and where.

Mrs Lawrence caught her looking at the fish kettle on the sideboard. 'You're from William's people,' she said. 'The north country. The mist-belt. I knew that landscape during the war, slightly. The Letaba valley. The scale of it changes you. People in Cape Town think the mountain is the definitive experience of scale. But they would think differently if they had stood in the north at flood.'

'My mother's kitchen floor was lye,' Ruth said. She had not intended to offer it. It came from somewhere below the management she was performing on herself, surfacing before she had decided to let it. 'She scrubbed it every Tuesday. The wood went white.'

Mrs Lawrence looked at her directly. 'That kind of country teaches a different arithmetic,' she said. She did not explain what she meant. She did not ask Ruth to.

After dinner the group moved back to the drawing room. Ruth drifted, carried by the room's current, towards the french doors that opened onto the lower veranda. They were slightly open, the cold coming through the gap in a purposeful stream, and she stood near it without choosing to, drawn by the cold in the same way she was always drawn by exits.

Thomas Lawrence was on the veranda.

She could see him through the glass of the open door, standing at the railing with his back to the room, the mountain above him entirely absent in the dark, only its pressure felt in the quality of the down-wind, the specific cold of stone at altitude coming off it. His hands were on the railing on either side of the water glass. The posture was not relaxed. It was the posture of someone gripping something to stay still.

She had been watching him for perhaps thirty seconds when he turned. She had not moved away in time. He had seen her.

After a moment he said, 'Are you fond of the dark?'

Ruth pushed the door open further and stood in its frame. The cold was immediate on her face. 'I find it quieter,' she said.

He absorbed this. 'Come out, then. If you don't mind the cold.'

She did not mind the cold. The veranda was wide, the flagstone damp and slightly slippery with it, and the mountain pressed close against the sky above the house, not visible but wholly present, the way a room is present even in the dark. The wind was direct here, not the building-deflected version that reached the drawing room, but the whole Cape Doctor coming down off the rock face without interruption. It found her collar immediately and the gap between her coat and her neck.

'They've had me going to a man in Wynberg,' Thomas said, his hands still on the railing, not looking at her. He spoke in the flat, reading tone of a person describing something that concerned someone else. 'Three months. It is, apparently, proceeding well.' He lifted the water glass and set it down. 'I behaved oddly at dinner. I don't like people to worry without information.'

'You ate your dinner and didn't talk much,' Ruth said. 'In my experience, that's not always odd.'

He made a sound through his nose that might have been a laugh. He looked at the dark of the garden. 'MacLeod,' he said. 'North country.'

'The lowveld. Tzaneen district. The Letaba valley.'

'I don't know the north. I've spent my life south of the Hex River. The Karoo occasionally. The Boland.' He turned the water glass on the railing. Its base made a quiet grinding sound on the iron.
