'The men I served with came from everywhere. Paarl. Aberdeen, Scotland. One man from the Caprivi Strip. We shared a tent for four months. He could name every tree on the strip and I couldn't name one.' He was quiet for a moment. The wind moved through the laurels in the lower garden, a slow, collective giving. 'El Alamein,' he said. He said it simply and exactly, as if it were a place you could point to on a map, and that was answer enough. He did not say it again.

Ruth stood beside him at the railing with her hands on the cold iron. She had been in the proximity of this before. Not the same experience: she did not know what he was carrying. But she knew the unexploded quality of a person who had been somewhere and come back holding something the visible world had no container for. The convent had carried it in the older sisters, in some of the girls, in the way certain silences were a different substance to other silences, denser, more specifically shaped, requiring a particular quality of stillness from the people near them. She had learned, in the convent, to be still in that particular way. Not empty. Present, and without demand.

She did not say anything. She looked at the dark of the garden and the darker mass of the mountain above the house and the small specific lights of the lower city far below, each one burning in a window where someone was still awake, and she waited.

'You know the high country at night,' Thomas said.

'I know the escarpment,' she said. 'From the farm in the dark you could hear things that had no names. Not animals. The rock. The cooling. Something in the geology.'

He looked at her. 'The Triassic,' he said, with quick, alert interest, the tone of a man for whom knowledge of this kind was a relief, an alternative register to the other knowledge he was managing. 'The escarpment is Triassic sandstone with a Pre-Cambrian basement beneath it. Very old. When you hear the dark there you're hearing something four hundred million years deep.' He paused. 'I read geology sometimes. It helps with the scale of ordinary things.'

'Yes,' Ruth said.

The drawing-room door opened behind them. Gillian looked out.

'Father. Mrs Rawlings is asking.'

'I know,' Thomas said. He looked at Ruth once, briefly, with the same direct and unperforming gaze she had first caught across the drawing room. Then he went in.

Ruth followed him after a moment, the cold still on her face. The drawing room's warmth was immediate and complete. She stood with the last of her sherry, which had gone warm, and looked at the fire. Across the room, the young man in the white jacket was collecting glasses from the mantelpiece with the same measured purpose with which he had moved through the entire evening, his back to the conversations, his purpose unaltered, the room's noise running around him without meeting him.

By ten o'clock the gathering had thinned. Morrison went first, his departure registering as a specific easing of something in Ruth's chest that she did not attempt to account for. The Rawlings twenty minutes later, Mrs Rawlings pressing a card into Ruth's hand for a Schumann series at the School of Music before Ruth had agreed to attend it. Pearce remained, turned towards Gillian with a focussed, considerate attention that Gillian appeared to be calmly assessing.

Mrs Lawrence found Ruth by the bookshelf.

'You must come again,' she said. The conviction in it was the same as at the beginning of the evening. 'William will be glad to know you came. He worries very quietly, which is the kind that costs the most.' She looked at Ruth in the measured way she had looked at her all evening, the gaze of a woman who reads rooms accurately. '"The kind of girl who reads everything in the room," he said. I thought that was a careful description from a man who is not given to them.'

Ruth said goodnight and found her coat on the stand. The woman in the soft apron was not there, only the stand and the hall's warmth and the sound of washing-up from beyond the kitchen door at the corridor's end, a rhythmic, muffled sound: water and crockery, the ring of a pan set on the drying rack, steady and unhurried.

She stood in the hall with her coat over her arm for a moment.

Gillian telephoned for a taxi from the hall telephone and stood waiting with Ruth in the hallway's warmth, neither of them talking much, the gramophone still audible through the closed drawing-room door.

'You were good with him,' Gillian said.

Ruth looked at her.

'On the veranda.' Gillian turned the telephone's cord between two fingers once and released it. 'Most people try to talk him back into the room. You didn't.'

The taxi sounded its horn at the bottom of the drive. Ruth said goodnight and went down the veranda steps.

The drive was fully dark now, the lamplight from the gate posts reaching only partway up the gravel. Her boots made their sound on the pale stones. She was perhaps halfway down when she stopped.

The upper storey of the house was lit in one window. The room above the dining room. A figure stood at the glass, not quite resolved, the interior light making the glass partially reflective, showing her the top of the dark garden and the mountain's mass above the roofline. The figure was still. It might have been Thomas. The light was behind it and the night was in front of it and she could not be certain of what she was seeing, only that someone was standing in a lit room on the upper floor while everyone else was still below.

She stood on the gravel in the cold with the wind coming down off the mountain. The shape in the window did not move.

She went through the gates.

Chapter 11: A Door Left Ajar

The room was above a bookshop in Gardens, reached by a narrow staircase that smelt of damp paper, floor polish, and the oranges someone had left to ripen in a chipped enamel bowl on the landing. Ruth had followed Judith because Judith had said there would be music, and because the word music had seemed safer than the other things Judith did not say.

At the top of the stairs the door stood open by the width of a hand.

Inside, the lamplight was low and yellow. Women sat on the floor with their backs against shelves, their shoes set aside, their stockings dark at the knee where the city rain had touched them. A gramophone turned in the corner. The voice coming from it was American, too smooth for the roughness of the room, but nobody seemed to mind. Smoke lifted in thin blue ropes and flattened against the ceiling.

Judith took Ruth's coat and hung it behind the door as if she had done it often enough to know which hook would hold. 'You may stay near me,' she said.

Ruth heard the permission inside the sentence and also the warning.

A woman in a man's white shirt looked up from the table where she was cutting lemons into a bowl of cheap gin. Her hair was dark and cut close against the nape. She had a small scar near the corner of her mouth, pale as fishbone. 'This is Ruth?'

'This is Ruth,' Judith said.

The woman did not offer her hand at once. She looked first at Ruth's shoes, then at the damp hem of her coat, then at her face. There was no cruelty in it. There was a practical weighing, as with Sarah in the Cohen dining room, only here the measurement did not place Ruth in service or in debt. It asked whether she could be trusted with a room.

'Clara,' the woman said.

Her hand was warm from the lemons. Ruth felt the sting of citrus on her own knuckles afterwards.

The talk moved oddly. Names arrived without surnames. Places were mentioned by corners rather than addresses. A girl from medical school spoke of a lecture in which a professor had compared obedience to health, and the room laughed with a quickness that did not feel light. Someone had brought Malay pastries wrapped in newspaper. Someone else had a flask hidden behind a stack of second-hand law books. The ordinary things of student life were all present, but each seemed to have been turned a little away from inspection.

Ruth sat on a cushion near Judith's knee. She held a cup she did not drink from and watched Clara lean across the table to light another woman's cigarette. The movement was small. Thumb, flame, the cup of one hand protecting the match. The other woman bent towards the light and stayed there a second too long. Nobody remarked on it. Nobody looked away.

The heat rose into Ruth's face before she understood what had caused it.

From the street below came the bell of a tram, then men's voices, then the hard clip of police boots on the wet pavement. The room changed without moving. A shoulder tightened. One woman reached for the gramophone and lowered the sound by a fraction. Clara set the matches down.

The boots passed.

Afterwards, laughter returned, but it had a seam in it. Ruth thought of the convent corridors at night, the learned art of hearing authority before authority saw you. She thought of Miss Pryce's pale eyes, of Eleanor's gold cigarette case, of Sarah at the kitchen table with smoke lying around her face. Different rooms. The same training in caution.

'You are very quiet,' Clara said.

Ruth looked down at the cup. A lemon seed floated against the rim.

'I am trying to understand the rules.'

'There are rules for outside,' Clara said. 'Inside we mostly try not to die of them.'

No one laughed that time.

Later Judith was called to the landing by a knock in the coded rhythm everyone seemed to know. Ruth found herself alone by the shelves with Clara, who was sorting a pile of pamphlets into two stacks. One stack carried names. The other did not.

'Do you always sort people like papers?' Ruth asked.

Clara smiled without showing her teeth. 'Only when papers can get people arrested.'

Ruth touched the spine of a book beside her. Greek tragedies, water-stained.

'Judith says you read old dead men.'

'They are safer than living ones.'

'Not always.'

The words sat between them, neither joke nor confession.

When Ruth left, the rain had stopped. The pavement shone under the lamps and the bookshop window held her reflection in pieces. Judith walked beside her without asking what she thought. Near the tram stop Ruth realised she had left her gloves upstairs. It would have been simple to go back.

She stood with her bare hands in her coat pockets and watched the lit window above the shop.

The day gave the scene no ceremony. In the Gardens room above the bookshop, a chipped enamel bowl of oranges seemed to gather the pressure of coded desire and the risk of being seen into one small surface. Ruth watched it until the rest of the room arranged itself around the watching. Judith tests Ruth before she opens the door, asking whether she understands that names can become evidence. The old farm habit in her wanted to reduce the matter to work: lift, fold, carry, answer when spoken to. Cape Town would not be reduced. It kept asking for forms of attention that had no place in Lena's kitchen and no protection in the residence rules.

Judith was there in the edge of it, not always speaking, sometimes merely altering the air by standing too close to the truth. Ruth felt the moment through the body first: the tightness at the base of the thumb, the dry place behind the tongue, the way sound from the street arrived thinned by glass or weather. When someone did speak, the words did not settle the scene. They opened another part of it. That was what frightened her most. A life could widen and still give no instruction for how to stand inside it.

What Ruth noticed first was not the danger but the ordinary object beside it. In the Gardens room above the bookshop, a gramophone needle seemed to gather the pressure of coded desire and the risk of being seen into one small surface. Ruth watched it until the rest of the room arranged itself around the watching. Elspeth speaks of the scholarship she lost after a warden's report, and the room goes still around the story. Chapter 12: Names for Hunger

For three days Ruth avoided the street with the bookshop.

She took the longer road down from campus, past the hospital wall and the eucalyptus trees that shed bark in strips like old skin. She told herself it was because the road was quieter. She told herself the tram came more reliably from that stop. She told herself many things and watched each explanation grow thin before it reached the end of the sentence.

At night the room at General Botha Hall held the cold as if it had been instructed to do so. The stone sill sweated. Her blankets smelt faintly of soap and dust. She lay on her back and heard the other girls moving behind their doors, water in basins, a laugh quickly covered, the soft thud of a shoe dropped onto the floor.

Her body remembered the lamplit room before her mind allowed it.

Clara's hand around the match. The close dark hair at the back of her neck. The other woman bending towards the flame. The hush when the police boots passed below.

Ruth turned onto her side and pressed her wrist against her mouth.

The convent had given many names to the body's disobedience. The words had been hard and clean, made to fit into confession like coins into a slot. Temptation. Sin. Occasion. Disorder. They had been spoken by women who wore wool in summer and looked at girls as though girlhood itself were a field that required fencing.

None of the words fitted the room above the bookshop.

In Greek tutorial, Arbuthnot asked her to translate a line from Euripides. Her mouth did the work, the old grammar rising from habit, but the sentence changed as she spoke it. Hunger became lack. Lack became want. Want became something with hands.

'Careful, Miss MacLeod,' he said, not unkindly. 'The word is not shame.'

The class rustled. Eleanor Stratford-Reid looked up from her notes. One of Connie Hargreaves's pearl earrings flashed as she turned her head by a fraction.

Ruth corrected the translation. Her voice sounded usable. It did not sound like hers.

Afterwards Miriam found her by the library steps and handed her a paper packet. 'Koeksisters. Fatima says you are getting thin in the wrong way.'

'What is the right way?'

'With intention.'

Ruth opened the packet. Syrup stuck to her fingers. The sweetness was sharp with spice, the coconut rough against her tongue. She ate one too quickly and felt the sugar move through her like a small, private rescue.

Miriam watched her. 'You went somewhere with Judith.'

Ruth folded the paper over the remaining pastry.

'People talk,' Miriam said. 'At university they pretend talk is thought because it wears better shoes.'

'What do they say?'

'Nothing yet worth repeating.'

Below them the campus lawns held their clean green line against the road. A gardener bent beside a flowerbed, his hat low, his hands black with damp soil. Two students walked past him discussing liberty in voices large enough for the steps to hear.

Miriam's face changed when she looked at them. Not much. Enough.

'There are places,' she said, 'where a person can breathe because the room has decided to pay the price. Then someone from outside notices the window is open.'

'Is that what the room was?'

'You tell me.'

Ruth could not.

That Friday she went back to Sarah's kitchen by way of the Cohen apartment. Judith had invited her for supper and Mrs Cohen had accepted her presence with the same dry efficiency with which she accepted the arrival of bread. After the plates were cleared Ruth found Sarah by the back step, cigarette unlit between her fingers.

'You look like somebody stole your shadow,' Sarah said.

The kitchen smelt of onions, brisket fat, and the starch from boiled napkins. Ruth stood near the sink.

'How does one know if a thing is wrong because it is wrong, or because everyone has put a fence around it?'

Sarah looked at her then. The glance was not soft. It was careful.

'Depends who built the fence.'

'And if God built it?'

'People put God's name on many things they are too ashamed to sign themselves.'

Ruth felt the sentence enter her body without asking permission.

Sarah struck the match at last and bent to the flame. Smoke came from her mouth in a thin, tired line. 'You don't ask me that question unless you already standing on the wrong side of somebody's gate.'

Ruth heard footsteps in the passage and moved back before anyone saw them too close together. Sarah did not move. That, too, was a kind of answer.

Later, in her room, Ruth opened William's letter. He wrote of Anna sleeping better, of Lena saying little, of a dry spell over the northern hills. There was a pressed leaf inside the page, brittle and brown, from the lemon tree near the kitchen door. It broke when Ruth touched it.

The day gave the scene no ceremony. In the library, the residence room, and Sarah's kitchen, a Greek grammar seemed to gather the pressure of religious residue pressed against bodily recognition into one small surface. Ruth watched it until the rest of the room arranged itself around the watching. Arbuthnot corrects Ruth's translation and gives her a word that sounds too close to hunger. The old farm habit in her wanted to reduce the matter to work: lift, fold, carry, answer when spoken to. Cape Town would not be reduced. It kept asking for forms of attention that had no place in Lena's kitchen and no protection in the residence rules.

Miriam was there in the edge of it, not always speaking, sometimes merely altering the air by standing too close to the truth. Ruth felt the moment through the body first: the tightness at the base of the thumb, the dry place behind the tongue, the way sound from the street arrived thinned by glass or weather. When someone did speak, the words did not settle the scene. They opened another part of it. That was what frightened her most. A life could widen and still give no instruction for how to stand inside it.

What Ruth noticed first was not the danger but the ordinary object beside it. Ruth watched it until the rest of the room arranged itself around the watching. Miriam reads Ruth's silence and refuses to let her hide behind scholarship work. Chapter 13: On the Mountain

The path began kindly.

That was the first deceit of it. A brown ribbon through fynbos, a few stone steps cut into the lower slope, the morning still cool enough for Ruth to believe her borrowed confidence might last. The cousins walked ahead with the light-footed impatience of people who had been given a mountain in childhood and had never considered that it might refuse them.

'Only to the saddle,' Philip Lawrence called back. 'Nothing heroic.'

Ruth smiled because the others smiled. The word heroic had already made a small wound.

Behind them Cape Town opened in layers: roofs, roads, the harbour cranes, the pale run of sea beyond the docks. From the path the city looked ordered and almost tender, its violences folded flat by distance. District Six was only colour from here. Hanover Street no more than a line. The university sat on its slope as if knowledge could be arranged by stone and height.

The path narrowed after the first bend.

Rock rose on the left. On the right the ground fell away in scrub and broken stone, not far at first, then far enough for Ruth to feel the space beside her as a pull against the ribs. She put one hand to the rock. It was warm already, rough under her fingers. A lizard vanished into a crack.

'You all right?' Audrey's daughter, Margaret, asked.

'Yes.'

The word came out too quickly.

They climbed. The wind changed. It came down from the upper face in hard, irregular breaths that pushed at skirts and lifted grit into the eyes. Ruth kept her gaze on Philip's boots, then on the stone immediately before her own. Boots, stone, hand. Boots, stone, hand. The pattern worked until the path broke across a sloping sheet of rock and the drop appeared all at once.

There was too much air.

Her knees took the knowledge before her mind did. They softened, then locked. Her hand scraped across the rock, skin catching. Below, the city shifted as if someone had lifted it slightly from the earth. The harbour flashed. A tram bell sounded impossibly thin.

'Ruth?' Margaret said.

She could not answer.

The convent returned in the body. Not memory as picture, but the old corridor narrowing around the throat. The whitewashed wall. The staircase after punishment. The voice behind her telling her not to make a display. She tasted iron.

Philip came back along the ledge, careless and concerned in equal measure. 'It's only a traverse. Don't look down.'

There were instructions that showed a man's whole education in one phrase.

Ruth moved one foot. The sole slid on fine gravel. Stone rattled away below her and kept falling long after it should have stopped. Margaret reached out. Ruth recoiled from the hand, then hated herself for it.

Cloud moved over the sun. The mountain's colour changed from honey to pewter. Higher up, someone shouted. The wind took the words apart.

'We should turn back,' Margaret said.

'Nonsense,' Philip answered, too fast.

The next gust struck them sideways. Margaret stumbled. Philip caught her arm, and in that movement his own boot lost purchase. He did not fall far. A yard, perhaps less, down to a lower lip of rock where a bush stopped him. But the sound he made was not the sound of sport. It was short and stripped of breeding.

Ruth went flat to the stone before anyone told her to.

The world reduced itself. Her cheek against grit. Her fingers finding a crack. Margaret crying Philip's name. The smell of crushed buchu sharp beneath her hand. She crawled because standing was no longer possible and because crawling belonged to no class code she had ever been taught. The rock tore the skin through her stocking. She did not feel it until later.

Philip lay below them, one knee twisted, face grey with the first arrival of pain. The bush held his jacket. Beyond him the slope dropped through loose stone to a place the eye refused to measure.

'Ruth,' he said.

For the first time since she had known him, he used her name without ornament.

She unfastened the scarf at her throat. Judith's scarf. She had meant to return it weeks ago and had failed and now its expensive silk slid through her bleeding fingers as she tied one end around the branch nearest Philip and the other around her wrist. It was foolish. It was too little. It was what she had.

'Do not pull unless I tell you,' she said.

Her voice sounded low. Practical. From somewhere below panic.

Margaret stared at her.

'Call down the path,' Ruth said. 'Now.'

The next minutes had no proper order. A man from another party appeared above them. Rope came. Philip swore once, then bit the word off. Ruth held the scarf until her fingers cramped and the silk cut into her skin. When hands finally pulled Philip up, the branch sprang back and struck her arm.

Afterwards they sat behind a boulder where the wind could not quite reach. Philip shook. Margaret cried without sound. Ruth looked at her own knees and saw blood soaking through wool.

The city below had not changed.

On the way down no one spoke of courage. Philip leaned between two men and kept his eyes on the path. Margaret walked close to Ruth, close enough that their sleeves touched. The scarf was ruined, dark with blood and green stain, its hem torn where the rock had bitten it.

At the lower gate, where the path widened and the first houses took them back into the ordinary world, Philip tried to laugh.

'Well,' he said, 'that was a little more than the saddle.'

Ruth looked past him to the mountain face, to the ledge already invisible from below.

The day gave the scene no ceremony. In the mountain path above the city, Judith's silk scarf seemed to gather the pressure of class performance turning into bodily peril into one small surface. Ruth watched it until the rest of the room arranged itself around the watching. Philip treats the climb as sport and Ruth hears the condescension hidden inside cheerfulness. The old farm habit in her wanted to reduce the matter to work: lift, fold, carry, answer when spoken to. Cape Town would not be reduced. It kept asking for forms of attention that had no place in Lena's kitchen and no protection in the residence rules.

Philip was there in the edge of it, not always speaking, sometimes merely altering the air by standing too close to the truth. Ruth felt the moment through the body first: the tightness at the base of the thumb, the dry place behind the tongue, the way sound from the street arrived thinned by glass or weather. When someone did speak, the words did not settle the scene. They opened another part of it. That was what frightened her most. A life could widen and still give no instruction for how to stand inside it.

What Ruth noticed first was not the danger but the ordinary object beside it. In the mountain path above the city, loose gravel seemed to gather the pressure of class performance turning into bodily peril into one small surface. Ruth watched it until the rest of the room arranged itself around the watching. Margaret lends Ruth a pin for her hair, the small kindness making refusal harder. The old farm habit in her wanted to reduce the matter to work: lift, fold, carry, answer when spoken to. Cape Town would not be reduced. It kept asking for forms of attention that had no place in Lena's kitchen and no protection in the residence rules.

Margaret was there in the edge of it, not always speaking, sometimes merely altering the air by standing too close to the truth. Ruth felt the moment through the body first: the tightness at the base of the thumb, the dry place behind the tongue, the way sound from the street arrived thinned by glass or weather. When someone did speak, the words did not settle the scene. They opened another part of it. That was what frightened her most. A life could widen and still give no instruction for how to stand inside it.

Chapter 14: The City's Dark Grammar

Sarah was stopped outside the railway station with a parcel of white shirts under one arm and Mrs Cohen's silver cake knife wrapped in newspaper in her bag.

Ruth saw the thing begin before she understood it. Two constables near the entrance, one young and pink-faced beneath his cap, the other older, bored in the practised manner of men who know the day will obey them. Sarah had been moving quickly, head down against the wind, when the older one stepped into her path.

'Papers.'

The word was small. The effect was not.

People adjusted themselves around the scene. A man selling newspapers lowered his voice. A white woman with a child took three steps sideways and became interested in the timetable board. The station did not empty. It continued, which was worse.

Sarah set the parcel down carefully.

Ruth crossed the floor before she had decided to move. The sound of her shoes on the tiles seemed foolishly loud.

'She works for the Cohens in Sea Point,' Ruth said.

The young constable looked at her then, taking in the university coat, the gloves, the whiteness that made intervention possible and dangerous in a different way. The older one held Sarah's pass between two fingers.

'And you are?'

Ruth felt the trap of the question. Name, residence, scholarship, warden, conduct. A whole machinery waited behind the ordinary request.

'A friend.'

Sarah did not look at her.

The older constable opened the newspaper around the cake knife. Silver flashed. His mouth moved as if humour had occurred to him, but he did not let it become sound.

'Nice friends you have.'

'It is Mrs Cohen's,' Sarah said. 'For sharpening. She ask me to take it.'

'She asked,' he corrected.

The correction landed harder than the suspicion. Ruth saw Sarah's jaw move once, back teeth meeting. She remembered the kitchen, the cigarette, the sentence about fences with God's name on them.

'I can telephone Mrs Cohen,' Ruth said.

'Can you.'

It was not a question.

Behind them a train exhaled steam. The platform filled with white vapour and coal smell. For a moment the whole scene blurred: Sarah's black coat, the constable's hand, the cake knife bright on the newspaper, Ruth standing with nothing in her possession except a voice the room was prepared to hear.

The older man returned the pass at last. Slowly. He put the knife back in the paper and handed it to Sarah as if giving alms.

'Next time carry a note.'

Sarah took the parcel. 'Yes, baas.'

The word struck Ruth in the mouth though she had not spoken it.

Outside, the wind pushed grit along the kerb. Sarah walked fast towards the tram stop. Ruth followed, then stopped when Sarah turned.

'Don't,' Sarah said.

'I only wanted to help.'

'You wanted him to know you are nice.'

The sentence left no room.

A tram clanged at the corner. Sarah looked towards it and then back at Ruth, her face shut against weather and witness.

'If you help me, you ask first. If you stand next to me, you stand where I put you. I am not one of your Greek women who wait for a white girl to understand tragedy.'

Ruth felt heat move up her neck.

'I am sorry.'

Sarah's eyes narrowed, not accepting, not refusing.

'Sorry is cheap when the other person pays.'

The tram arrived. Sarah climbed aboard without looking back.

That afternoon campus argued about the Cape Coloured vote in the language of principle. In the student hall David Cohen spoke with anger bright along the edge of every sentence. Eleanor Stratford-Reid asked a question so beautifully phrased that several boys nodded before the harm inside it had settled. Arbuthnot spoke of constitutional difficulty, of precedent, of the need for intellectual steadiness.

Ruth sat near the back and watched hands lift and fall.

The room was full of people who could say disenfranchisement without tasting station dust.

When Miss Pryce stopped her in the residence passage that evening, she did not mention the station. She mentioned propriety.

'Several students have observed that you are keeping unusual company, Ruth.'

The corridor smelt of boiled cabbage and damp wool. A girl laughed behind a closed door and then lowered her voice.

'Yes,' Ruth said.

Miss Pryce waited for more. Ruth gave her nothing.

The warden folded her hands. 'A scholarship girl must be careful not to mistake sympathy for conduct.'

Ruth looked at the small silver cross at Miss Pryce's throat, lying correctly against dark cloth.

'Conduct for whom?'

The question hung there, plain and dangerous.

The day gave the scene no ceremony. In the station, the student hall, and the residence passage, Sarah's pass seemed to gather the pressure of the city making private conscience public into one small surface. Ruth watched it until the rest of the room arranged itself around the watching. Ruth sees Sarah's hands become still before the constable finishes his first question. The old farm habit in her wanted to reduce the matter to work: lift, fold, carry, answer when spoken to. Cape Town would not be reduced. It kept asking for forms of attention that had no place in Lena's kitchen and no protection in the residence rules.

Sarah was there in the edge of it, not always speaking, sometimes merely altering the air by standing too close to the truth. Ruth felt the moment through the body first: the tightness at the base of the thumb, the dry place behind the tongue, the way sound from the street arrived thinned by glass or weather. When someone did speak, the words did not settle the scene. They opened another part of it. That was what frightened her most. A life could widen and still give no instruction for how to stand inside it.

Chapter 15: Men After War

Colonel Lawrence took Ruth to the MOTH hall on a Thursday because Audrey had said he ought not go alone.

The hall stood behind a bowling green, low-roofed and brown, with a flagpole out front and a row of clipped shrubs that had been made to submit to symmetry. Inside, the air smelt of beer, old wool, pipe smoke, and the sweet rot of polish rubbed for years into boards walked by men who had survived enough to make ceremony necessary.

There were photographs on the wall. Men in desert kit. Men beside lorries. Men with their arms around one another before youth had left their faces. Beneath the photographs stood the same men twenty years older than their own mouths, their laughter too sudden, their silence too expertly kept.

'Good chaps,' Colonel Lawrence said.

Ruth looked at his hand around the glass. Two fingers trembled, then stopped when he gripped harder.

A man called Harris told a story about Italy. Everyone laughed before the end, as if they knew where to enter the performance. Another man sat alone under a framed certificate and polished the bowl of his pipe with a handkerchief until the briar shone.

The talk moved around danger but seldom entered it. Weather. Rations. A quartermaster's incompetence. A mule that would not die. Places became jokes. Names became initials. The dead were present in the spacing between sentences.

Ruth stood near the tea table because Audrey had asked her to help with cups. The urn spat and hissed. Her sleeve smelt of steam. She watched Colonel Lawrence cross the room with the same easy body he used at dinner, but here the ease had another use. It kept something upright.

'Your uncle was good at maps,' Harris said to Ruth.

'He is not my uncle.'

'Near enough for family harm.'

The man grinned, then seemed to hear himself and looked away.

Later the Colonel found her by the door to the yard. The evening had gone blue outside and the bowling green lay flat as baize under the last light. He had taken off his tie. Without it his neck looked older.

'They think I remember more than I do,' he said.

Ruth waited.

'One is expected to carry the correct version of events. For morale. For widows. For oneself, I suppose.'

He lifted his glass, found it empty, and looked faintly surprised.

'And the incorrect version?'

He smiled then, not at her. 'That arrives in pieces. At breakfast. In church. When Audrey drops a tray.'

From inside came a burst of singing. Men's voices, uneven, loyal to a tune some of them could no longer hold. Ruth felt the sound against her sternum.

She thought of William at Pietersburg station, hat in hand, saying too little because more would have broken the platform apart. She thought of Kit's face at the train window. She thought of fathers and uncles and wardens, all the people who had been given forms for damage and expected to live inside them.

Colonel Lawrence put the empty glass on the sill.

'Do not let Philip take you above the saddle again,' he said.

'He was hurt too.'

'That was not what I meant.'

His eyes met hers then. For one second the polish of the British house was gone and something exhausted stood in its place, bare and almost childlike.

Audrey called from inside. 'Thomas?'

He turned at once, the old manner falling back over him.

'Coming, darling.'

The day gave the scene no ceremony. In the MOTH hall behind the bowling green, a beer glass seemed to gather the pressure of male damage held inside ritual into one small surface. Ruth watched it until the rest of the room arranged itself around the watching. The hall arranges grief into flags, photographs, and the clean lines of military memory. The old farm habit in her wanted to reduce the matter to work: lift, fold, carry, answer when spoken to. Cape Town would not be reduced. It kept asking for forms of attention that had no place in Lena's kitchen and no protection in the residence rules.

Colonel Lawrence was there in the edge of it, not always speaking, sometimes merely altering the air by standing too close to the truth. Ruth felt the moment through the body first: the tightness at the base of the thumb, the dry place behind the tongue, the way sound from the street arrived thinned by glass or weather. When someone did speak, the words did not settle the scene. They opened another part of it. That was what frightened her most. A life could widen and still give no instruction for how to stand inside it.

What Ruth noticed first was not the danger but the ordinary object beside it. Chapter 16: The Uncle at the Beach

They found Colonel Lawrence at Muizenberg three days after he left the house without his hat.

Audrey had spent the first day calling it one of Thomas's walks. On the second day she sat by the telephone with her gloves on. By the third, the skin beneath her powder had taken on the colour of cooled ash, and Philip drove Ruth down to the beach because Margaret had refused to go.

The sea was the dull green of bottle glass. Wind drove sand along the promenade in low, stinging sheets. Men stood by the bathing boxes with newspapers folded under their arms and looked towards the surf as though the horizon might apologise.

They found him near the railway bridge, sitting on an upturned crate beside a man who sold bait. His jacket was buttoned wrongly. One shoe had lost its lace. He was holding a piece of driftwood and marking lines in the sand that the wind erased almost as soon as he made them.

Philip stopped the car too hard.

'Stay here,' he said.

Ruth did not.

Colonel Lawrence looked up when they approached. For a moment he seemed pleased to see them, then wary, then bored by the effort of placing them correctly.

'The tide table is wrong,' he said.

Philip crouched before him. 'Uncle Thomas.'

'No, no. They printed it for Simon's Town and sent it here. Entirely different water.'

The bait seller moved away, giving them the courtesy of distance without pretending not to listen.

Ruth stood with sand hitting her stockings and watched Philip try to gather a man who did not want to be gathered. The Colonel's hands were filthy. Beneath one nail was a thin black crescent of tar. He had been crying or the wind had made his eyes stream. It did not matter which.

'Audrey is worried,' Philip said.

'Audrey worries correctly. It is one of her accomplishments.'

'You must come home.'

The Colonel looked past him to the water. 'Home requires witnesses.'

Philip's face tightened. He had no language for this version of the man who had taught him to tie knots, polish shoes, and never let a woman carry anything heavier than flowers.

Ruth stepped closer. 'Colonel Lawrence.'

He looked at her then, and something steadied. 'The mountain girl.'

'Yes.'

'You did well on the rock.'

Praise, arriving late and from wreckage, had a strange weight. Ruth did not know where to put it.

'Mrs Lawrence is waiting,' she said.

'Poor Audrey.'

He stood suddenly, too fast. Philip caught his elbow. The Colonel shook him off with surprising violence.

'Do not handle me like a parcel.'

People turned. A child stopped eating an ice cream and stared.

Ruth saw the humiliation move through Philip first as anger, then as fear. She saw how quickly family love could become management when public eyes arrived. She had seen versions of it in Pietersburg, in corridors, in the careful way William spoke around Lena when Anna was ill.

Colonel Lawrence dropped the driftwood. The wind rolled it once.

'I only needed the sea to be large enough,' he said.

Nobody answered.

On the drive back, the Colonel slept in the front seat with his head against the window. Philip drove too fast. Ruth sat in the back and watched Muizenberg fall away, the beach huts shrinking into their painted row, bright little houses holding the line against a sea that had no use for them.

At Bellevue, Audrey met them at the door. She had changed her dress. Her lipstick was exact. She touched Thomas's sleeve and said, 'There you are,' as if he had been late for tea.

He looked at her hand.

Ruth carried his other shoe from the car because no one else had noticed it lying under the seat. At the threshold she stopped. Sand had spilled from it onto her palm.

The day gave the scene no ceremony. In Muizenberg beach and the Lawrence house, an upturned crate seemed to gather the pressure of escape mistaken for collapse into one small surface. Ruth watched it until the rest of the room arranged itself around the watching. Audrey names the absence as a walk until the word can no longer carry the day. The old farm habit in her wanted to reduce the matter to work: lift, fold, carry, answer when spoken to. Cape Town would not be reduced. It kept asking for forms of attention that had no place in Lena's kitchen and no protection in the residence rules.

Colonel Lawrence was there in the edge of it, not always speaking, sometimes merely altering the air by standing too close to the truth. Ruth felt the moment through the body first: the tightness at the base of the thumb, the dry place behind the tongue, the way sound from the street arrived thinned by glass or weather. When someone did speak, the words did not settle the scene. They opened another part of it. That was what frightened her most. A life could widen and still give no instruction for how to stand inside it.

What Ruth noticed first was not the danger but the ordinary object beside it. Ruth watched it until the rest of the room arranged itself around the watching. Philip drives too fast because speed gives his worry a respectable shape. Chapter 17: Ink and Judgment

Ruth began the letter three times.

Dear Father was too narrow because William had never been the whole of home, however gently he had tried to stand there. Dear Mother made the page stiffen beneath her hand. Dear all of you was cowardice. She crossed it out so heavily the nib tore the paper.

Rain moved down the window in fine slanting lines. The room smelt of wet wool and ink. Her fingers were stained at the joints. Outside in the passage someone was practising a song for the residence concert, the same phrase rising and failing, rising and failing, until the repetition entered Ruth's teeth.

She wrote of lectures first. It was the safest lie because it was true.

Arbuthnot has given me extra reading. The work is difficult but I am managing.

She wrote of the mountain and stopped before the ledge. She wrote of the Lawrences and left out Muizenberg. She wrote that Cape Town was beautiful and then looked at the word until it became useless.

The letter from William lay open beside her.

Anna asks whether the sea is as large as the stories say. I told her it is larger, because I did not know what else to tell her. Your mother says you have not written in a proper hand for some weeks. She worries that university makes girls clever in the wrong places.

The last sentence was William's wording of Lena's accusation, softened until the bone barely showed.

Ruth dipped the pen.

Tell Anna the sea is not one thing. Some days it is metal. Some days it is green glass. Some days it is only noise behind fog.

That much she could send.

She could not send Clara's name. She could not send the room above the bookshop, Sarah stopped at the station, Judith's scarf torn on the mountain, Miss Pryce's voice saying conduct as if conduct were a room with one door and a guard at it. She could not send the fact that every week made the farm both dearer and less possible.

So she wrote carefully around the life she was living.

Halfway through the second page, a note was pushed under her door.

Miss MacLeod. Please attend the warden's office tomorrow at nine regarding correspondence received.

No signature. Miss Pryce did not require one.

Ruth stared at the paper until the ink at the edge of her own letter dried in a raised ridge.

At nine the next morning the warden's office smelt of furniture polish, cold tea, and the closed air of rooms where girls were corrected. Miss Pryce sat behind the desk with two envelopes before her. One was Ruth's last letter home, redirected and returned by William with a note in his hand on the back. The other was addressed to the residence.

'Your mother has written to me,' Miss Pryce said.

Ruth did not sit until told.

'She expresses concern about your associations, your religious observance, and the tone of your recent letters.'

Recent letters. Plural. Lena had been collecting the evidence in silence.

'My mother does not know my associations.'

'That appears to be part of her concern.'

Miss Pryce opened the second envelope and removed a sheet. She did not read aloud. She did not need to. Her eyes moved over the page and left Ruth to imagine the words.

'A scholarship is not merely a financial arrangement,' she said. 'It is a trust.'

Ruth heard the old structure close around the new city. Farm, convent, residence. All of them with registers. All of them with women who believed supervision was love in its proper clothes.

'May I see the letter?'

'It was written to me.'

'About me.'

Miss Pryce folded the page.

'That distinction is less clear than you imagine.'

By evening Ruth had written another letter, not to William, not to all of them, but to Lena.

Mother,

If you want to know what I am becoming, you must ask me and not strangers.

The sentence sat on the page with more heat than grammar. Ruth read it twice. She should have torn it up. Instead she wrote on, each line plainer than the last, until the paper held more of her than she had meant to give.

At the post box the rain had stopped. Water dripped from the blue metal lid onto her cuff. She held the letter for one more second.

The day gave the scene no ceremony. In Ruth's residence room and Miss Pryce's office, an opened envelope seemed to gather the pressure of family language becoming institutional evidence into one small surface. Ruth watched it until the rest of the room arranged itself around the watching. Ruth tries to write about Cape Town and leaves more unsaid than said. The old farm habit in her wanted to reduce the matter to work: lift, fold, carry, answer when spoken to. Cape Town would not be reduced. It kept asking for forms of attention that had no place in Lena's kitchen and no protection in the residence rules.

William was there in the edge of it, not always speaking, sometimes merely altering the air by standing too close to the truth. Ruth felt the moment through the body first: the tightness at the base of the thumb, the dry place behind the tongue, the way sound from the street arrived thinned by glass or weather. When someone did speak, the words did not settle the scene. They opened another part of it. That was what frightened her most. A life could widen and still give no instruction for how to stand inside it.

Chapter 18: Rumour

The rumour did not arrive as a sentence.

It came first as a space at the dining table. Two girls who usually left their books on the chair beside Ruth moved them before she sat down. Vivienne Hartley smiled too brightly and asked whether she had enjoyed Gardens on Saturday. Connie Hargreaves said nothing at all, which was worse because Connie knew how to spend silence with care.

By noon the library had changed shape.

Ruth felt it in the longer pause before the librarian stamped her books. In the way Eleanor Stratford-Reid looked at her and then past her, granting neither recognition nor release. In the whisper that stopped when Judith came through the door.

Judith found her in the Classics stacks between Homer and a shelf of Loeb editions with faded red spines.

'Who saw you?'

Ruth's hand tightened around the book she was holding. 'Saw me where?'

'Do not waste time.'

The directness was a kindness and a wound.

Ruth thought of Saturday: the bookshop room, the gramophone, Clara's hand at the small of her back for the span of two steps because someone had spilled gin near the table. The landing afterwards. Clara close enough for Ruth to smell lemon, tobacco, and rain in her hair. A laugh from the street. A face below, turned up towards the window.

'Vivienne,' Ruth said.

Judith closed her eyes for one second.

'She was with Eleanor.'

'Of course she was.'

The book in Ruth's hand began to hurt her fingers.

By afternoon the thing had reached Miss Pryce. Not in a crude form. Nothing that could be answered because nothing had been openly asked. The warden sent for Ruth and spoke of late evenings, unsuitable rooms, the danger of political gatherings, the care owed by a young woman to her name.

'My name is mine,' Ruth said.

Miss Pryce looked almost sad. 'That is a very modern misunderstanding.'

There were forms on the desk. Scholarship review forms. Conduct endorsement. The machinery had been there all along, waiting for a reason to move.

'I have received further concern from your family,' Miss Pryce said.

Lena's letter had travelled faster than love ever had.

Ruth left the office with the air too thin in the corridor. At the stairwell Eleanor stood speaking to Connie, one gloved hand on the banister. Vivienne hovered half a step behind them. When Ruth approached, Eleanor smiled.

'Ruth. I hope you are well.'

Every word was polished clean of accusation.

'Did you tell her?' Ruth asked Vivienne.

Vivienne's mouth opened. She looked first at Eleanor.

That was answer enough.

Eleanor's expression cooled by no visible degree and yet the corridor felt colder. 'One must be careful where one is seen. Especially when one is already vulnerable to misunderstanding.'

'Misunderstanding.'

'Yes.'

Ruth stepped past her. Their sleeves touched. Eleanor moved back as if preventing a mark.

Outside, the mountain was hidden by low cloud. The campus lawns were bright from rain, too green, almost theatrical. Ruth walked without a destination until she found herself at the tram stop below the hill.

Clara was waiting there.

She wore a dark coat and no hat. Rain had caught in her hair.

'Judith told me,' Clara said.

'Then everyone knows.'

'Not everyone. Enough.'

The tram rails shone between them. Ruth wanted Clara to say it was nothing. Wanted her to say the room would hold, that names were only sounds, that the world outside could not enter if the door remained shut. Clara did not offer any of those lies.

'You can deny it,' Clara said.

'Can you?'

Clara looked towards the road. A police lorry passed slowly, two men in the back under a canvas cover, their faces half hidden.

'No.'

The word was flat. Not brave. Exact.

Ruth felt the old machinery starting inside her, the part trained to survive by pleasing the person with the ledger. It offered explanations. It offered small betrayals dressed as prudence.

The tram arrived. People gathered behind them, close enough to hear any sentence spoken wrongly.

Clara touched Ruth's sleeve, only once, two fingers at the cuff. 'Choose where you can live with yourself.'

Then she stepped onto the tram by the rear door and did not look back.

Ruth stood on the pavement as the car pulled away. In the upper window Vivienne's reflection appeared for a second beside her own, pale and watching.

The day gave the scene no ceremony. In the residence dining room, the library, and the tram stop, a moved book seemed to gather the pressure of private life entering public speech into one small surface. Ruth watched it until the rest of the room arranged itself around the watching. The rumour first appears as a chair no longer saved at breakfast. The old farm habit in her wanted to reduce the matter to work: lift, fold, carry, answer when spoken to. Cape Town would not be reduced. It kept asking for forms of attention that had no place in Lena's kitchen and no protection in the residence rules.

Vivienne was there in the edge of it, not always speaking, sometimes merely altering the air by standing too close to the truth. Ruth felt the moment through the body first: the tightness at the base of the thumb, the dry place behind the tongue, the way sound from the street arrived thinned by glass or weather. When someone did speak, the words did not settle the scene. They opened another part of it. That was what frightened her most. A life could widen and still give no instruction for how to stand inside it.

Ruth watched it until the rest of the room arranged itself around the watching. Eleanor makes concern sound like clean linen folded over a blade. The old farm habit in her wanted to reduce the matter to work: lift, fold, carry, answer when spoken to. Cape Town would not be reduced. It kept asking for forms of attention that had no place in Lena's kitchen and no protection in the residence rules.

Miss Pryce was there in the edge of it, not always speaking, sometimes merely altering the air by standing too close to the truth. Ruth felt the moment through the body first: the tightness at the base of the thumb, the dry place behind the tongue, the way sound from the street arrived thinned by glass or weather. When someone did speak, the words did not settle the scene. They opened another part of it. That was what frightened her most. A life could widen and still give no instruction for how to stand inside it.

Chapter 19: The Letter That Cannot Be Recalled

Ruth knew the letter was wrong before the ink dried.

That did not stop her.

The room was too small for the weather inside it. Rain beat at the glass. The lamp smoked. Miss Pryce's note lay on the desk beside Lena's last page, both papers creased where Ruth had gripped them too hard. From the passage came the low murmur of girls preparing for bed, ordinary and merciless.

Mother,

You have written to my warden as if I were still a child to be reported.

The line looked almost calm. That was its danger.

She wrote of the station at Pietersburg, of Lena not coming. She wrote of Anna in the doctor's room, of William's hands on his hat, of Esther Goldman left on the platform with her face turning smaller as the train pulled away. She wrote of the convent because Lena had never understood that discipline was not the same as care. She wrote of Cape Town, though not enough, never enough. Sarah at the station. District Six. The room above the bookshop. Clara's name hovered at the edge of the page and Ruth held it back, not out of shame now but protection.

Then Lena's word from the last letter came back.

Unnatural.

Ruth set the nib to the paper again.

If what I am is unnatural to you, then you have loved an idea and called it your daughter.

The sentence was too clean. It cut even while lying still.

She should have stopped there.

Instead she wrote one more page, and in that page truth became something harder than truth needed to be. She wrote because she was tired of being softened by William's mediation. She wrote because Miss Pryce had made the residence another convent. She wrote because every room in her life seemed to contain a woman ready to guard the door and call it love.

At the post box she stood in the rain with the envelope under her coat.

There was still time.

A boy came running past with newspapers under sacking, shouting the evening edition. A tram scraped round the corner. Somewhere nearby a woman laughed, then coughed. The city continued, indifferent to the small violence of a posted letter.

Ruth lifted the lid and let it fall from her hand.

The next morning she woke before the bell with her mouth dry.

For two days nothing happened. That was the cruelty of distance. The letter had left her and entered a sequence of bags, trains, sorting rooms, hands. It moved north without remorse. She attended lectures, translated lines, nodded when Miriam asked whether she had eaten. The letter was already elsewhere and still it occupied the room.

William's telegram came on Friday afternoon.

LETTER RECEIVED. COME IF POSSIBLE. ANNA ASKING FOR YOU. WILLIAM.

Only seven words beyond the names. No accusation. No plea. That was how she knew something had broken.

She packed without telling Miss Pryce until the last possible hour. Judith came and sat on the bed while Ruth folded clothes.

'Do you want me to come to the station?'

'No.'

Judith nodded. Her hands were clasped hard enough to whiten the knuckles.

'Then take money.'

'I have enough.'

'You do not.'

She placed an envelope beneath Ruth's hairbrush and left it there.

At the station, Sarah appeared at the edge of the platform carrying a paper parcel.

'Mrs Cohen sent bread,' she said.

'Did she?'

'No.'

Ruth took it. The paper was warm.

Sarah looked at the train, then at Ruth. 'When you go back to a house that hurt you, don't go in asking it to become another house. Look first.'

The whistle blew.

Ruth climbed aboard with the bread against her ribs and the telegram folded inside her glove. As the train pulled out, Cape Town slid backwards in smoke and wet stone.

The day gave the scene no ceremony. In Ruth's room, the post box, and the Cape Town station, Lena's letter seemed to gather the pressure of one letter becoming irreversible into one small surface. Ruth watched it until the rest of the room arranged itself around the watching. Ruth rereads Lena's word until the paper seems to give off cold. The old farm habit in her wanted to reduce the matter to work: lift, fold, carry, answer when spoken to. Cape Town would not be reduced. Chapter 20: Winter North

The north received her with dust under the cold.

Winter had taken the colour out of the grass without softening the light. The veld lay flattened and pale beyond the station, the thorn trees holding their branches like old injuries. Ruth stepped down from the carriage at Pietersburg with Sarah's parcel gone, Judith's money hidden in her stocking, and the smell of coal smoke in her hair.

William was waiting by the far post.

For a second she saw only what had altered. The deeper lines beside his mouth. The loose hang of his coat. The way his hand rose and then stopped before touching her shoulder.

'Ruthie.'

The old name nearly undid her because he said it without claim.

They drove first to the doctor's rooms, not the farm. Anna was there for review, William said, and Lena had refused to bring her to the station. He said this carefully, as if each word had to pass through a gate.

The waiting room smelt of disinfectant, dust, and stale magazines. Anna sat by the window with a blanket over her knees though the sun had reached her feet. Her face had thinned. Her eyes were still Anna's, which made the rest harder.

'You came from the sea,' Anna said.

Ruth knelt before her. 'Yes.'

'Is it loud?'

'Always.'

Anna considered this. 'I should like something that does not stop.'

William turned towards the wall.

At the farm the kitchen door stood open. Lena was inside, rolling dough with hard, even strokes. She did not come out. Ruth stood in the yard and looked at the house as if it belonged to another family with similar furniture.

The lemon tree was smaller than memory had allowed. The stoep boards had warped. A tin basin near the step held grey water with a skin of flour across it. Everything was known and slightly wrong.

'Your room is made up,' Lena said when Ruth entered.

No embrace. No question about the passage. The letter lay between them without needing to be produced.

Supper was mealie bread, cold mutton, and tea strong enough to darken the cup. Anna ate little. William spoke of the cattle. Lena corrected him twice on small matters that did not require correction. Ruth watched the lamp throw light onto the table, onto the knife marks, onto the place where her own hand had once been small enough to fit in the oval burn near the edge.

'Your warden says you have become difficult,' Lena said.

William's fork stopped.

'Not tonight,' he said.

'When, then? When she has gone back to whatever life she thinks is freedom?'

Ruth put her cup down carefully. 'I came because Anna asked.'

'Anna asks for many things she cannot have.'

Anna looked at her plate.

The old house settled around them. Wood, tin roof, distant animal movement, the slow tick of the clock. Ruth had imagined return as a wound reopening. It was stranger than that. The wound had been there all along. The house had merely kept its shape around it.

That night she lay in her childhood bed beneath blankets that smelt of sun and storage. Through the wall she heard Lena moving in the kitchen long after the others had slept. A chair scraped. A cupboard opened. The small rituals of a woman refusing rest.

Ruth turned her face to the wall.

The day gave the scene no ceremony. In Pietersburg station, the doctor's rooms, and the farm kitchen, a station post seemed to gather the pressure of return as estrangement into one small surface. Ruth watched it until the rest of the room arranged itself around the watching. The platform at Pietersburg carries the memory of departure without offering its old shape back. The old farm habit in her wanted to reduce the matter to work: lift, fold, carry, answer when spoken to. Cape Town would not be reduced. It kept asking for forms of attention that had no place in Lena's kitchen and no protection in the residence rules.

William was there in the edge of it, not always speaking, sometimes merely altering the air by standing too close to the truth. Ruth felt the moment through the body first: the tightness at the base of the thumb, the dry place behind the tongue, the way sound from the street arrived thinned by glass or weather. When someone did speak, the words did not settle the scene. They opened another part of it. That was what frightened her most. A life could widen and still give no instruction for how to stand inside it.

What Ruth noticed first was not the danger but the ordinary object beside it. Chapter 21: No Homecoming

In the morning Ruth tried to help with the washing and did everything in the wrong order.

Lena said nothing at first. That was worse than correction. She watched Ruth lift the copper lid too soon, watched her wring a shirt badly, watched her hang Anna's nightdress where the shade would hold it damp. Each mistake was small and domestic and final.

'Cape Town has not taught you use,' Lena said at last.

Ruth pinned the wet cloth to the line. Water ran down her wrist into her sleeve.

'It has taught me other things.'

'Yes. I have read.'

The letter again. Everywhere.

Anna sat under a blanket on the stoep, shelling peas into a bowl. She did it slowly, thumb to seam, pod opening, peas dropping one by one with soft green taps. Ruth carried the basket to her and sat on the step.

'You smell different,' Anna said.

'Do I?'

'Like rain on stone.'

Ruth smiled because Anna had given Cape Town a kinder name than anyone else in the house.

From the yard Lena watched them. Ruth felt the look but did not turn.

The day lengthened. William took Ruth to inspect a fence line because he did not know how else to speak without interruption. They walked through winter grass. The land was dry enough to make each step audible. Far off, cattle moved like dark scraps against the pale slope.

'Your mother is frightened,' he said.

'Of me?'

He looked at the fence wire. 'Of losing the shape she understands.'

'That is not the same as losing me.'

'No.'

He bent to tighten a loop of wire with pliers. His hands were slower than they had been when she was a child.

'Did you read the letter?' she asked.

The pliers stopped.

'She showed me enough.'

'And?'

'And I wished I had been quicker all these years.'

The answer went through her more painfully than accusation would have done.

They walked back after sunset. The farmhouse windows shone yellow in the darkening yard. Once that light would have meant entry. Food. Noise. The right to be expected. Now it looked staged, as if someone had lit the lamps for a traveller passing on the road.

Inside, Lena had taken Ruth's Cape Town books from her suitcase and set them on the kitchen table.

Greek grammar. A notebook. Clara's pamphlet with no name on it, folded into the back cover.

Ruth saw it at once.

Lena's face was white around the mouth.

'What is this?'

William came in behind Ruth. Anna's chair creaked in the next room.

'A paper,' Ruth said.

'Do not treat me as stupid.'

The pamphlet lay between them. It was nothing in itself, only a few pages about police powers and the right to refuse certain questions. But beside the rumours, beside Miss Pryce's letter, beside Lena's fear of all unnamed hungers, it became whatever Lena needed it to be.

'You opened my suitcase.'

'In my house.'

The phrase struck the table and stayed there.

Ruth looked around the kitchen. The scrubbed boards. The stove. The blue enamel bowl. The small cracked tile near the hearth where she had once hidden a button because she believed lost things could be kept safe if buried close to warmth.

My house.

Not yours.

The room did not change. That was the devastation. It remained entirely itself while refusing her.

Ruth picked up the pamphlet and placed it back inside the book.

'I understand,' she said.

Lena laughed once, sharply. 'You understand nothing.'

But Ruth did. At last. Not all of it, not kindly, not in a way that could save anyone.

The day gave the scene no ceremony. In the washing line, the fence line, and the farm kitchen, wet linen seemed to gather the pressure of memory separating from belonging into one small surface. Ruth watched it until the rest of the room arranged itself around the watching. Ruth's hands fail at the washing because the body has learned another order. The old farm habit in her wanted to reduce the matter to work: lift, fold, carry, answer when spoken to. Cape Town would not be reduced. It kept asking for forms of attention that had no place in Lena's kitchen and no protection in the residence rules.

Lena was there in the edge of it, not always speaking, sometimes merely altering the air by standing too close to the truth. A life could widen and still give no instruction for how to stand inside it.

Ruth watched it until the rest of the room arranged itself around the watching. Lena turns usefulness into judgement and makes domestic work a test Ruth cannot pass. The old farm habit in her wanted to reduce the matter to work: lift, fold, carry, answer when spoken to. Cape Town would not be reduced. Chapter 22: The Last Argument

The argument began over Anna's shawl.

That was how such things survived long enough to become catastrophic. Not with a grand accusation, but with wool misplaced over the back of a chair. Anna had asked for the blue one. Ruth found it folded in Lena's room and brought it out. Lena saw it in Ruth's hands and the air altered.

'You go through everything now.'

Ruth stopped beside the table. 'Anna is cold.'

'Anna has been cold for years. You notice it when it suits you.'

William rose from his chair. 'Lena.'

'No. She writes filth from Cape Town, arrives with her papers and her city airs, and now she will perform care in front of us.'

Anna was in the next room. The door stood partly open. Ruth could see the wheel of her chair, one hand resting on the blanket, fingers curled inward.

'Do not speak about me as if I am not here,' Ruth said.

'You have made sure you are never here.'

The blue shawl hung from Ruth's hands. It was soft from years of use, the edge darned in two places. She remembered Anna wearing it in the doctor's room at Pietersburg, remembered promising to write, remembered the train pulling away from Esther Goldman and Kit and William on the platform while Lena remained at the farm by choice.

'You did not come to the station,' Ruth said.

Lena's face tightened.

'Your sister needed me.'

'I needed you.'

The sentence had no force in the room. It was too late to be a plea.

Lena put both hands flat on the table. 'You needed discipline. You needed gratitude. You needed to remember what kind of family you came from before those people filled your head.'

'Which people?'

'Do not make me say it.'

'Say it.'

William moved between them, but Ruth stepped aside.

Lena looked towards the door where Anna sat unseen and listening. Her voice lowered. That made it worse.

'The Jews with their money and clever talk. The Coloured woman you shame yourself over. Those girls in rooms without decency. The university men who teach daughters to despise their mothers. All of them.'

Ruth felt each word arrive, distinct as stones placed in a line.

'Sarah has shown me more decency than many respectable women.'

'Sarah.' Lena said the name as if Ruth had brought dirt into the kitchen.

'Yes. Sarah.'

'And the other one?'

Silence entered so quickly even William seemed to feel the cold of it.

Ruth did not speak Clara's name. Not here. Not for Lena to hold in her mouth.

'There it is,' Lena said. 'You cannot even answer.'

'Because you do not want an answer. You want a confession.'

'You are my daughter.'

'Then look at me.'

The room stilled.

Lena's eyes moved over her face, her dress, the ink stain near her thumb, the city still clinging to her in ways no washing could remove. For one breath Ruth thought she might do it. Might see the child and the woman together, not as betrayal but as fact.

Lena looked away first.

Something inside Ruth ceased its labour.

William said her name. Very softly. Ruth did not turn.

'I cannot live inside your fear and call it home,' she said.

Lena's mouth trembled, but no softness came. 'Then do not call this home.'

Anna made a sound in the next room. A small sound, animal and broken.

William went to her.

Ruth placed the shawl on the chair. Her hands were steady now. That steadiness felt less like strength than weather after a roof has gone.

'I will leave in the morning.'

No one answered.

The day gave the scene no ceremony. In the farm kitchen and Anna's half-open doorway, Anna's blue shawl seemed to gather the pressure of mother and daughter reaching the summit of rupture into one small surface. Ruth watched it until the rest of the room arranged itself around the watching. The shawl begins the argument because care has become contested ground. Chapter 23: What Remains of Family

William found Ruth in the shed before sunrise.

She had gone there because it still smelt of him as he had been when she was small: oil, leather, dust, the clean sourness of metal tools. A hurricane lamp burned on the bench. Her suitcase stood open on an upturned crate. She was folding slowly, more slowly than the task required.

'You will miss the first train if you keep making those stockings so obedient,' he said.

It was nearly a joke. Nearly.

Ruth looked up. He had two mugs of coffee in one hand and a brown paper packet in the other. He set one mug beside her and stood with his back against the bench.

For a while they drank without speaking.

'Your mother slept in the chair,' he said.

Ruth closed the suitcase.

'That is not an apology.'

'No.'

He rubbed one thumb over the handle of his mug. 'I do not know how to make the true thing useful.'

The lamp flame moved in the draught beneath the door.

'Say it anyway.'

William looked at her then. His face held all the years in poor order.

'I should have come between you sooner. Not last night. Years ago.'

The words did not repair. They did not even console. But they entered the shed and remained there, real enough to stand beside the oil tins and the coils of wire.

Ruth nodded once.

'Anna wants you,' he said.

Anna was awake in the front room, wrapped in the blue shawl. Lena was nowhere visible. The kitchen had been scrubbed before dawn, the table still damp, as if cleanliness could erase what had happened on it.

Ruth knelt beside Anna's chair.

'You are going,' Anna said.

'Yes.'

'For always?'

Ruth could not lie to her. Not entirely.

'For now.'

Anna touched Ruth's sleeve. Her fingers were light, the nails pale crescents.

'I heard the leaves.'

'Last night?'

'When Mother said it. The tree sounded like paper.'

Ruth lowered her head until her forehead rested against Anna's hand.

'I am sorry you heard.'

'I hear many things. People forget because I sit.'

The sentence held no complaint. That made it harder.

Anna asked for the sea again. Ruth described it badly because no description could carry the force of water to a room so dry. She told her about the gulls fighting over scraps near the harbour, about the way fog could remove the mountain by degrees, about District Six in the morning when bread smell came out of doors before people did.

Anna listened with her eyes half closed.

'Write it down for me,' she said.

'I will.'

'Not tidy.'

Ruth almost smiled. 'No. Not tidy.'

At the door William carried her suitcase to the motorcar. Lena stood in the passage, one hand on the frame of the kitchen door. Her face looked older in the unkind morning light. Ruth waited for a word. Any word. Lena looked at the suitcase, then at Ruth's coat, then at the floor between them.

'Your gloves are on the chair,' she said.

Ruth took them.

That was all.

On the drive to the station, William did not try to explain Lena. He did not ask Ruth to write more kindly. He did not say time would settle things, which was mercy of a kind.

At the platform he handed her the brown packet. Inside was biltong, wrapped in wax paper, and beneath it a small envelope.

'For when you have decided where to go,' he said.

The train came in with a long iron sigh. Ruth climbed aboard, then turned at the door.

William lifted his hand.

The day gave the scene no ceremony. In the shed, Anna's room, and the station platform, two mugs of coffee seemed to gather the pressure of love surviving where belonging fails into one small surface. Ruth watched it until the rest of the room arranged itself around the watching. William brings coffee to the shed because apology needs an object in his hands. The old farm habit in her wanted to reduce the matter to work: lift, fold, carry, answer when spoken to. Cape Town would not be reduced. It kept asking for forms of attention that had no place in Lena's kitchen and no protection in the residence rules.

William was there in the edge of it, not always speaking, sometimes merely altering the air by standing too close to the truth. Chapter 24: To Krugersdorp, Alone

Cape Town looked different when Ruth returned.

The mountain was still there, blunt against the morning, and the harbour still gave off its mixture of salt, coal, rope, and fish, but the city no longer presented itself as an answer. It had become a place with rooms in it. Some had held her. Some had measured her. Some had asked a price before she knew she was paying.

At General Botha Hall, Miss Pryce received the news of Ruth's withdrawal from residence with administrative regret.

'You understand this may affect future endorsements.'

'Yes.'

'And your scholarship?'

'I will finish the term from outside residence.'

Miss Pryce folded her hands. 'That is irregular.'

Ruth looked at the neat desk, the conduct forms, the vase of flowers placed where no scent could trouble anyone.

'So am I.'

She packed the room in less than an hour. There was not much to take. Books. Two dresses. The torn remnant of Judith's scarf, washed but still faintly marked by the mountain. Anna's request written on a scrap of paper. William's envelope, unopened until she reached the tram.

Inside was money. Not enough for ease. Enough for a beginning. There was also a note.

Do not spend your life proving you can endure what should never have been asked of you.

W.

She read it twice, then folded it into the Greek grammar.

Judith met her outside the residence gates. Miriam came too, carrying a paper bag of food because Miriam trusted bread more than sentiment. Clara waited a little apart beneath the plane trees, hat low, hands in her coat pockets. Sarah arrived last, breathless from the tram, with a parcel wrapped in newspaper and tied with string.

'For the road,' Sarah said.

'I am not leaving today.'

'Then for the room before the road.'

No one asked where she would sleep. They had already arranged it. A room in Woodstock for two weeks, then perhaps a cheaper place near the station. Practicalities moved around Ruth with a care that did not make speeches.

That evening, in the room in Woodstock, Clara placed a clipping on the table. A school outside Krugersdorp required an assistant teacher for Latin and English. Temporary post. Board not included. Salary small. Respectability uncertain.

'It is far enough,' Clara said.

Ruth read the notice. Krugersdorp. The name had a hard inland sound, gold dust and railway iron, neither farm nor sea. Not refuge. Direction.

'You think I should go.'

'I think you should choose before other people choose the smaller life for you.'

Outside, children shouted in the lane. Someone was frying fish. The room smelt of paraffin, damp plaster, cumin from downstairs, and the wool of Ruth's coat drying over a chair.

'And you?' Ruth asked.

Clara looked towards the window. The glass reflected both of them poorly, two women broken by the wavering pane.

'I am not a station you can arrive at.'

The sentence hurt because it was honest.

Ruth accepted the post three days later. She wrote to William first. Then to Anna, with the sea described badly and truthfully, not tidy. She did not write to Lena. Not yet.

On the morning she left Cape Town, the station was full of steam and voices. Judith kissed her cheek quickly and pretended she had not. Miriam pressed food into her bag. Sarah stood with her arms folded and inspected Ruth's coat as if checking whether the city had done its work properly.

'Keep your back straight,' Sarah said.

Ruth smiled then, a small thing. 'Or the rock gets heavy for everyone.'

Sarah's face changed, not softening, exactly. Recognising.

Clara did not come to the platform until the whistle had already blown. She handed Ruth a book through the carriage window. No inscription. A pressed lemon leaf inside.

'For the train,' she said.

The guard called. Doors closed along the platform. The train pulled once against its couplings, then again, and the city began to move backwards.

Ruth stood in the corridor until the platform thinned. Judith's scarf remnant was in her pocket. William's note was in the grammar. Anna's page lay in the small notebook on her lap, waiting for the sea.

Beyond the last signal, the mountain held its shape for a long time.

The day gave the scene no ceremony. In Cape Town, Woodstock, and the departure platform, William's money envelope seemed to gather the pressure of costly self-direction toward Krugersdorp into one small surface. Ruth watched it until the rest of the room arranged itself around the watching. The city receives Ruth differently because she has stopped asking it to become home. The old farm habit in her wanted to reduce the matter to work: lift, fold, carry, answer when spoken to. Cape Town would not be reduced. It kept asking for forms of attention that had no place in Lena's kitchen and no protection in the residence rules.

Judith was there in the edge of it, not always speaking, sometimes merely altering the air by standing too close to the truth. Ruth felt the moment through the body first: the tightness at the base of the thumb, the dry place behind the tongue, the way sound from the street arrived thinned by glass or weather. When someone did speak, the words did not settle the scene. They opened another part of it. That was what frightened her most. A life could widen and still give no instruction for how to stand inside it.

What Ruth noticed first was not the danger but the ordinary object beside it.
